LOAN AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:              The Republic of Korea
Beneficiaries:         Korea Long-Term Credit Bank (KLB) and Korea Develop-
ment Bank (RDB) for the credit component; Government
agencies and other financial institutions for the
technical assistance component.
Amount:                US$222.0 million equivalent.
Terms:                 The proposed loan would be at the Bank's standard
variable interest rate plus commituent fee. The
credit component of the loan (US$220 million) for
KLB and KDB would have two separate amortization
schedules, neither exceeding 15 years, but conforming
substantially to the aggregate of the amortization
schedules applicable to the specified investment
projects financed out of the proceeds of the proposed
loan by each institution. The technical assistance
component (US$2.0 million) would have a fixed
amortization schedule, repayable in 15 years, including
3 years of grace.
Relending Terms:       The Government of Korea would relend the proceeds of
the credit component to KLB and KDB on the same terms
as the Bank loan, but with an administration fee of
0.05%. $120 million equivalent of the credit component
would be allocated to KLB, and RDB's portion would be
$100 million equivalent. KLB and KDB would onlend the
funds to their clients at a variable interest rate at
least 2% above the effective cost of the Bank loan
with maturities not exceeding 13 years including
grace periods of up to three years, except where
longer grace periods would facilitate commercial
cofinancing of subprojects. Subborrowers would
bear the foreign exchange and interest rate risks on
the loan.
Proceeds of the technical assistance component would
be onlent by the Government on the same terms and
maturity as-the Bank's loan to financial institutions
sending their personnel to project-supported training
activities.
Project Description:   The project constitutes the primary vehicle for Bank
involvement in financial sector reform in Korea. The
policy dialogue over the FY85-87 period would be based
on a Policy Letter from the Minister of Finance that
ITh docment has a resticted ditbution and may be used by ripients oy in the performance of I
th  ofiTa duies Its conutt may not otewis be dsiwed vithot Word Bank autw   I



outlines a monitorable program of reform through
decontrol of interest rates, reduction of directed
credit, commercial bank liberalization and supervision,
control of corporate indebtedness and equity market
development.
The project would provide credit to cover the foreign
exchange cost of industrial equipment imported by
private firms seeking to acquire, build, modernize
or replace productive assets. The median economic
rate of return estimated for subprojects approved
under the first Industrial Finance Project is 25Z,
and similar investment performance is expected under
the proposed project.
The project would also provide technical assistance
for the training of managers in private financial
institutions and in related Government agencies. The
project would also address ffiancial and institutional
issues related to KLB and RDB that were initiated under
previous projects involving these institutions.
Given KLB and KDB's record in utilizing Bank loans,
institutional risks in the proposed project are
minimal. The institutional arrangements for the
technical assistance component will require
careful supervision because of their innovative
nature and the number of institutions that are
expected to participate. The only significant
question is whether financial sector
reform would progress more slowly than expected
because of unforeseen changes in domestic and
international conditions. The Government remains
committed to the gradual implementation of far-
reaching financial reform; its cautious and consistent
approach appears likely to succeed.

KOREA
SECOND INDUSTRIAL FINANCE PROJECT
I. THE INDUSTRIAL SECTOR
1.01      Three features characterize Korea's industrial development since
1960: rapid growth, export orientation and successful diversification, and
debt dependence. At 15%, Korea's manufacturing sector growth rate ranks among
the highest in the world between 1960 and 1983. Korea's dynamic growth has
been led by exports, especially by manufactured exports. In 1962, Korea's
total commodity exports were US$55 million, while in 1983 they reached US$24.4
billion with manufactured exports accounting for over 93%. The structure of
manufactured exports has changed substantially from one based largely on light
industrial goods (e.g., textiles, wood products) to a range of products
including machinery, basic metals, and chemical products. For example, the
share of machinery in total exports increased from 9% in 1970 to 32% in 1983
while that of wood products declined from 11% to 0.3%.
1.02      Industrial growth and diversification have been financed largely by
debt. Korean corporations are highly leveraged. The inflation-adjusted
average debt-to-equity ratio among manufacturing enterprises, around 1.5:1, is
high by international standards. Korea's external debt has risen from $22
million in 1960 to over $43 billion in 1984, making it the fourth largest
borrower among developing countries.
1.03      Korea's remarkable industrial development during the 1960s was
associated with a generally dirigiste approach within which the Government
maintained comprehensive export incentives, an extensive system of guided,
concessional credit and substantial expenditure on human and physical infra-
structure investments. This approach was also considered appropriate for the
next stage of industrial development, and beginning about 1974 the Government
promoted a shift from light and simpLe industries to heavy and sophisticated
industries (e.g., shipbuilding, chemicals) on the assumption that this
anticipated the country's changing competitive advantage.
1.04      The interventionist approach did not meet with as much success after
1974, due partly to its inherent weaknesses and partly to the second oil shock
in 1979 and the subsequent world recession. The promotion of heavy and
chemical industries contributed to an increase in debt and excess capacity as
Korea sought to maintain investment rates in priority sectors despite sharply
higher oil prices and interest rates, and lower sales. Capacity utilization
in the manufacturing sector fell from 88% in 1978 to 732 in 1980, w:-th no
significant improvement in 1981 and 1982. Light industries had difficulties
obtaining funds and their development fell below planned targets and their
products slipped in export competitiveness. Between mid-1979 and 1981, GNP
was stagnant and real per capita income adjusted for terms of trade declined
by about 10Z.



-2-
1.05      These circumstances caused the Government to begin to question the
policy of choosing sectors to develop and of promoting them through large
infusions of cheap credit. The Government recognized that the economy is too
large and complex to be efficiently guided by direct controls. Consequently,
direct intervention is being gradually reduced in favor of direction from
incentives and market signals.
1.06      This change is reflected in the Fifth Five Year Plan (1982-86) and
in agreements made with the World Bank under the structural adjustment loans
of 1981 and 1983 and the Industrial Finance Project of 1983. Broadly speak-
ing, Government is now committed to policies of industrial, trade and finan-
cial liberalization. This approach and improved world economic conditions are
contributing to the recovery of the Korean economy. In the manufacturing
sector net profit as a percentage of sales rose from -1.1 in 1980 to 1.7 in
1983, and the ratio of net worth (equity) to total assets increased from 17%
to 22%. Prospects for further industrial development are bright. Value added
in manufacturing is expected to grow by 10% annually over the next several
years and a deeper and broader industrial structure should resuLt from efforts
to diversify exports and promote research and development.
II. FINANCIAL SECTOR
2.01      The Korean financial system has grown rapidly.  Average consolidated
assets of the system increased from W 9.6 trillion in 1975 to W 102.6 trillion
in 1984. With this growth have come changes in structure (Annex 5). Finan-
cial development during the 1970s included the rapid growth of a second-tier
market and the emergence and spread of new types of financial instruments.
FinanciaL Structure
2.02      The financial sector in Korea consists of three tiers or systems
differentiated largely by degree of government regulation: a heavily regula-
ted primary system composed of the Bank of Korea (BOK), commercial and specia-
lized banks that collect deposits and make loans, and development banks; a
less regulated secondary system of insurance, investment, finance and securi-
ties companies; and an unregulated tertiary system of informal lenders and
agents known as the curb market. Table 1 shows how the asset structure of the
regulated financial system changed between 1975 and 1984. (Similar data are
not available for the curb market.) The secondary system has grown rapidly
and has an asset market share equal to 27% (as compared with only 10% less
than a decade ago), while the market share of the BOK has slipped from 23% to
12% and that of commercial banks from 37Z to 30%. This realignment in
structure occurred because of the ability of most secona tier institutions to
raise deposits and lend at interest rates higher than those permitted banking
institutions, and the growing demand for financial services more flexible than
those traditionally provided by commercial banks. Recognizing that this
difference in regulatory treatment may be penalizing commercial banks to an
undesirable extent, Government has moved recently towards greater parity of
treatment.



-3-

2.03      The primary banking system is dominated by deposit money banks.
Seven private'-city banks" with a nationwide network of branches, ten private
local banks with regional operations, and more than 50 branches of foreign
banks account for 30Z of financial assets. The Government has increased the
autonomy and flexibility of commercial banks, and has allowed them to issue
certificates of deposit, undertake commercial paper and trust business, and
differentiate interest rates within a narrow band to reflect cost and risk
characteristics of their loans. Foreign bank branches experienced rapid
growth from a small base during 1975-84. Their role is likely to be further
enhanced by a recent measure allowing them parity in regulatory treatment with
domestic banks by 1986. Six specialized banks account for a further 20% of
total financial assets, their share having remained constant since 1975.
These banks were created by the Government to serve particular sectors such as
small industry, exporters, housing and agriculture.
2.04      Commercial and specialized banks account for approximately 70% of
total lending to the manufacturing sector in Korea. The bulk of their lending
is short-term. However, long-term equipment financing constitutes almost 40%
of the manufacturing sector lending of some specialized banks.
2.05      Development banks' market share is 11% and has remained roughly
constant. There are three such banks, the Korea Development Bank (KDB), the
Korea Long-Term Credit Bank (KLB), and the Korea Export-Import Bank. The bulk
of the Bank Group's industrial lending is channelled through KDB and KLB,
which are important sources of equipment finance for the manufacturing sector.



- 4 -
2.06      The second tier financiaL system contains savings and trust institu-
tions, insurance companies, venture capital firms, short-term finance com-
panies, and securities dealers. Each of these groups of institutions has more
than doubled its asset share over the last ten years, while the short-term
investment finance companies (SIFC) have more than trebled theirs.
2.07      SIFCs were established in 1972 as alternative channels for funds
that flowed through the curb market: 10 existed by 1974 and 32 were operating
by 1983. They raise deposits and lend at rates higher than those permitted
commercial banks, and deal in commercial paper. Their rapid growth is largely
due to this regulatory flexibility, and is reflected in the increase in the
ratio of funds loaned by SIFCs to funds loaned by commercial banks from 11 in
1974 to 40Z in 1983. Net profit ratios of SIFCs and commercial banks indicate
that the former are doing much better. Net profit to net worth was 12.0% and
2.0%, respectively, in 1983 and a similar pattern prevailed in earlier
years. The higher profitability of SIFCs is probably also due to their
relative freedom from Government regulation, especially in loan allocation.
SIFCs are able to orient their activities towards a seLected group of
customers, especially those that do business with the corporation(s)
affiliated with the finance company as shareholders.
2.08      The curb market is noninstitutional, composed of deposit takers,
lenders, agents and financial brokers whose activities are beyond supervision
by Covernment authorities. Recent estimates of its size range from W 1.0 to
1.5 trillion, approximately 6X of total domestic credit to the private
sector. This informal short-term finance market operates as a "safety valve"
by providing relief from constraints in the formal credit sector where rates
and voLumes are subject to government control. It also caters to high risk
business shunned by banks on commercial, legal or other grounds, and offers
anonymity and the prospect of higher returns to depositors willing to accept
the accompanying risk.
Development of Financial Instruments
2.09      Table 2 indicates how the use of financial instruments has changed
in recent years. Two trends are immediately obvious: (a) loans have declined
in relative usage while trade credit has become more important and (b)
commercial paper has become increasingly acceptable and popular although, in
relative terms, transaction volume remains low.   More detailed data reveal
rapid growth of the securities market, within which the bond and debenture
market has grown faster than the stock market.



5

2.10      The most significant development in the bond market over the past
decade has been the growing popularity of primary corporate bonds. The amount
outstanding grew at an annual compound rate of 77% between 1975 and 1982,
raising their share to 85Z of the bond market. By 1982 almost 97Z of corpo-
rate bonds carried bank guarantees. Guaranteed corporate bonds serve as an
indirect form of bank financing, substituting for loans that banks have been
unable to issue because of the slow growth in their deposits.
2.11      Secondary bond market transactions in 1982 totaled W 6,247 billion,
which is almost three times the amount of corporate bonds issued in that   -
year. The secondary market consists almost entirely of sale and purchase of
bonds under repurchase agreements. The period between the initiaL and the
reverse transaction averaged approximately four months in 1982-83.
2.12      The stock market has grown, but not so rapidly as other securities
markets. The total market value of shares dropped from 76X of total
securities in 1975 to 40Z in 1982. The composite stock price index rose from
89.7 in 1975 to 127.3 in 1982. Small investors have been discouraged by
transaction costs, the thinness of the market, and the variability of share
prices.  The total number of shareholders declined from 1 million in 1978 to
680,000 in 1982, as bonds and other assets became more attractive than stocks.
Government Controls and Intervention
2.13      The rapid development of the Korean economy was accompanied by
highly interventionist government policies. Strict government control over
resource allocation by the financial system was a major tool in development
strategy. Controls included restrictions on the establishment of new
financial institutions, ownership stakes in the banking sector and of develop-
ment banks (excluding KLB), detailed regulation of bank management and
staffing decisions, determination of interest rates, and selective credit
controls. While industrial growth was reliant on the private sector,



-6-
investment priorities were determined by the Government. The Government
committed resources to fund priority investments, including financing on
preferential terms channelled through development banks and deposit money
banks.
2.14      Subsidization of lending rates in proportion to investment priority
was widespread, created substantial price distortions and, to the extent that
some lending rates were fixed below deposit rates, eroded the profitability of
financial intermediaries. Narrow average margins between deposit and lending
rates made intermediaries dependent on subsidies channelled primarily through
the centraL bank. The two main programs of selective and subsidized lending
in the 1970s were the Bank of Korea's rediscounting facility for short-term
export credit, and the National Investment Fund (NIF) for Long-term equipment
financing. Interest subsidies attached to most programs not funded by the BOK
were removed with the interest rate reform of June 1982. These programs,
which were concentrated in the industrial sector, then accounted for about 20%
of total private sector domestic credit outstanding. Controls have also
covered resources mobilized independently by financial institutions, which
have been directed to specific subsectors such as small industries and
machinery manufacturing on terms and conditions specified by Government. In
aggregate, the Government controlled approximateLy 50% to 60% of total banking
resources.
2.15      As the disadvantages of the dirigiste approach became apparent when
the economy came under stress largely from external economic developments
(paras. 1.04-1.05), the approach was modified. The policy of the Government
is now to decentralize economic decision making in the real sector and to
reduce government intervention in finance so that market forces are able to
play a greater role in resource mobilization and allocation. The Government
is phasing the reform, which began in 1981, so that changes in interest rates
and policies do not have a destabilizing effect on the corporate sector, which
is still heavily indebted, and on the commercial banks, which have had low
profits and weak portfolio performance emanating, in part, from past govern-
ment policies. Deregulation of interest rates and reduction in intervention
is occurring gradually, while constraints on the banking and corporate sectors
are being addressed directly.
Financial Sector Issues
2.16      The objective of financial reform is:  to improve resource
allocation by decentralizing investment decisions so that entrepreneurs,
financial institutions and portfolio investors can respond more rapidly to
perceptions of risk and reward; and to enhance efficiency in intermediation as
financial institutions are subjected to greater competition through less
legislated specialization and the establishment of new institutions.
Considerable progress has been achieved in implementing reform.
2.17      As part of the dialogue on financial sector issues, the Government
provided the Bank with a "Letter of Development Policy for the Financial
Sector" dated April 14, 1983, which outlined the major elements of its reforms
and the steps proposed to implement them. A second Policy Letter has been



provided under the proposed project and is attached as Annex 1, and a summary
of measures to be taken and their expected economic results is contained in
Annex 2.
2.18      The Letter identifits the five main issues currently at the core of
the financial reform program: (a) decontrol of interest rates, (b) reduction
of directed credit, (c) commercial bank liberalization and supervision,
(d) control of corporate indebtedness, and (e) equity market development.
Equity market development was not addressed in the policy letter accompanying
the first Industrial Finance Project. Its inclusion here represents a growing
awareness of the favorable prospects for equity market development in Korea.
It also reflects increased scope for Bank collaboration with IFC's long-
standing involvement with the Korean equity market. The Letter outlines each
issue, the Government's objectives in dealing with it, and specifies measures
expected to be implemented through 1987. The Letter is the primary vehicle of
the financial sector dialogue between the Government and Bank in connection
with this project, and presents a series of initiatives subject to monitoring
by the Bank.
Decontrol of Interest Rates
2.19      Objective:  Interest rate decontrol should gradually permit interest
determination by market forces operating within the framework of monetary and
fiscal management by the Government. Shortcomings of government controlled
rates have included: substantial differentials between legislated lending
rates and non-legislated rates which led to excessive demand for credit at
legislated rates and artificial growth of the second tier and curb markets
where rates are substantially higher; and interest margins insufficient to
promote the independence of regulated financial intermediaries. In additdion,
subsidized credit can bias investment toward capital-intensive technology,
lead to reduced efficiency and to welfare losses that retard growth, and
concentrate credit in large firms at the expense of savers and of smaller
firms. As Long as controlled interest rates remain beLow market-determined
levels, interest rate decontrol will constitute a major area requiring
government action.
2.20      Progress in Interest Rate Decontrol.  In 1982 commercial bank
lending rates were sharply reduced and unified at 10% as part of a series of
actions to deal with inflation and economic problems arising from the second
oil shock, world recession and death of President Park. These measures have
proved generally successful; Korea has stabilized its economy and is recover-
ing welL from these dislocations. The dramatic decrease in the rate of
inflation has turned the 10% nominal rate into a positive real rate exceeding
7% in 1984. From this base, a range of controlled interest rates has been
developed.  A lending rate band of 10-10.52 for com ercial banks was
introduced in January 1984 with interest rate incentives for lengthening the
term structure of deposits. However, the half-percent band did not permit
sufficient rate differentiation to reflect risks and costs to lenders, and the
ceiling was increased to 11.5% in November 1984, and to 13.5Z in April 1985.
Similar increases have been made on several types of time and savings deposits
as part of the Government's long range plan for liberalizing the financial
sector.



8-
2.21      Outside the commercial banking sector, interest rate ceilings have
been less binding and financial markets are increasingly able to respond to
market forces within government guidelines. Rates on 90-180 day commercial
paper have been liberalized since June 1981, and yields on primary issues of
corporate bonds and bank debentures have moved very close to second tier
market rates ranging upward from 13Z. The Government has also allowed
development banks, including KDB and KLB, to lend longer-term domestic
currency funds at efft.ctive rates which approximate second-tier market rates
of interest. Curb market rates, incorporating a significant risk premium, are
reported to have dropped to a low of 1.2% per month, partly in response to
partial deregulation of non-bank financial instruments and lower inflationary
expectations. One effect of this dualistic market structure has been to slow
the growth of the deposit base supporting the commercial banks' lending at
controlled rates (para. 2.02).
2.22      The differential between administered and market rates has narrowed,
but the gradualist strategy has not yet fully-met its interest rate reform
objective, which is the freeing of interest rates or at least the raising of
ceilings to the point where they exceed market rates and therefore no longer
constrain these rates. The strategy was predicated on the assumption that
rates in the second tier market would decline at least to the level of con-
trolled commercial bank lending rates with good economic management manifested
in dramaticaLly lower rates of inflation. While inflation control objectives
have been achieved, with an increase of only 2.8% in the consumer price index
in 1984, rates of interest in the second tier market have remained above the
controlled lending rate band. This is due at least in part to rates in
international financial markets, and also reflects competition for funds
domestically. While in real terms the current rates are very high by
historical standards, controls still distort the structure of the financial
market and its ability to allocate resources efficiently.
2.23      Steps to be taken to decontrol interest rates will consist of direct
and indirect measures (Annex 1, paras. 6-13; Annex 2, Section A). Direct
measures consist of raising ceiling rates, so that over time the maximum
permitted levels are applied to very few transactions and can be abandoned
without material economic repercussions. Indirect measures may be politically
easier to implement, as suggested by Government's creative use of new instru-
ments carrying higher rates than traditional instruments. The introduction of
negotiable certificates of deposit, with a rate of 11% and a requirement that
banks lend 90% of the funds raised from these instruments at 12%, while the
commercial bank lending rate band was 10-10.5%, is an example of this type of
measure. Liberalization will require that rates on both loans and deposits
are increasingly determined by competition and by negotiation between those
supplying funds and those obtaining funds. The Government plans to keep
interest rates positive in real terms, reduce the gap between controlled rates
and market rates for similar transactions, and raise interest rate ceilings
specitied for commercial banks and other financial institutions through
1987. New instruments will continue to be introduced.



-9-
Reduction of Directed Credit
2.24      Objective:  The Government intends, through financial reform, to
reduce its direct role in the allocation of credit, which should enable finan-
cial intermediation to be more efficient. Directed lending programs and
selective credit controls have been the main tools of government intervention
in credit allocation. Of these, lending programs utilizing government funds
earmarked to support investment in specific firms and industries are the most
direct form of intervention. These are being considerably reduced as reform
proceeds.
2.25      Progress in reducing distortions from directed lending has involved
the phasing out of some marginal funds and, in June 1982, the raising of
interest rates on most remaining funds to the same level as the minimum
commercial bank lending rate. The National Investment Fund (NIF) and the
rediscounting of short-term export credits by the BOK remain the two main
instruments of directed lending (para. 2.14). Since 1982, the Government has
made NIF allocations on a sectoral basis, rather than targeting specific firms
as loan recipients, which has provided banks handling NIF loans more autonomy
in credit decisions. The volume of loans arranged through the NIF system was
reduced from W 733 billion in 1983 to W 506 billion in 1984. This was
accompanied by a reduction in utilization of resources from W 823 billion to
W 586 billion. What is even more encouraging is the fact that the bulk of
annual mobilization is now from loan repayments: net contributions dropped
from W 444 billion in 1983 to W 73 billion in 1984.- These trends are expected
to be maintained in the future with a view to eliminating the NIF over time.
2.26      Under the short-term export credit facility, bills discounted by
conmercial banks at their standard lending rate base of 1OZ are rediscounted
at 5%, with the rediscountable portion fixed at 70Z. Gains from rediscounting
contribute significantly to commercial bank revenues. This subsidy appears
increasingly unnecessary with liberalization of interest rates, increases in
commercial bank fees on guarantees, and access of foreign commercial banks to
BOK rediscounting facilities (para. 2.35). Part of these gains may be passed
on to commercial bank clients generating export paper through the banks'
competition for this type of business. The impact on exporters from reduction
in the rediscount subsidy would probably not be large. Government is
reviewing its rediscounting policy.
2.27      Steps to be taken include a continued decline in the flow of funding
to NIF. Eventual reduction in its total assets could be achieved by using
proceeds from maturing loans to make refunds to contributing institutions in
proportion to their cumulative payments into the Fund. It is also expected
that the activities of the NIF will be increasingly concentrated in KDB (para.
4.56) with the phasing out of commercial banks as conduits for_NIF lending
(Annex 1, paras. 14-15; Annex 2, Section B).
2.28      Selective credit controls have been used to direct more financing
towards small and medium industry. Interest rates, constrained by rate
ceilings, have not been allowed fully to perform an allocative role, which is
attempted instead by these controls. National city banks are expected to have
35% of their loan portfolios in credit to firms in these categories, while



- 10 -
regional commercial banks have been given a 50% target. Insurance companies
and other types of financial institutions also have been assigned similar
portfolio targets. Use of these indirect measures to influence credit
allocation is to be preferred over direct intervention which is embodied
primarily in the Small and Medium Industry Promotion Fund which lends at 9%.
This Fund is not expected to grow significantly. To achieve a high degree of
financial liberalization, special funds would have to be deemphasized and
selective credit controls would also have to be diminished. Selective credit
controls will require review as financial reform progresses and will be
monitored under the project. Interest rate decontrol (para. 2.23) should
reduce the advantages to be gained from selective credit control.
Commercial Bank Liberalization and Supervision
2.29      Objectives for the commercial banking system include the creation of
a sustainable, competitive market in banking services offered by strong
financial institutions (Annex 1, paras. 16-22; Annex 2, Section C). This
requires that bank profitability be maintained at levels adequate to reward
shareholders, increase capitalization and provide adequate reserves for bad
debt losses. These levels of performance do not appear to have been achieved
by Korean banks since the oil shock and world recession of the early 1980s.
2.30      Progress towards creating strong, competitive banks.  The commercial
banking system accounts for about 30% of the total assets of the Korean finan-
cial sector (Chapter II, Table 1). The five large nationwide "city banks"
played an important roLe in administering the directed credit system. With
liberalization they have been privatized, which is expected to increase their
competitiveness and efficiency. In an effort to control concentration, the
voting power of any single investor is limited to 8% of total shares
outstanding. Financial sector reform has included a change in the approach to
financial supervision. To reduce the role of the Government in the management
and operation of the commercial banks, all regulations governing internal
management such as personnel, organization and budgeting have been abolished
and regulations of business operations have been simplified. In addition to
these major changes, supervision is moving from reliance on prior authoriza-
tion by supervisory agencies to ex-post monitoring of performance in relation
to a simplified set of guidelines.
2.31      Commercial banks have also been authorized to undertake new
activities in competition with the nonbanking sector and to improve their
earnings. The Government has also licensed new financial institutions and
deregulated some financial activities to reduce legislated specialization and
market segmentation. The MOF is considering proposals by KDB and KLB to
diversify their sources of funds and improve their competitiveness. Govern-
ment actions have increased competition in finance, but they have also raised
longer-term issues concerning the limits, if any, that should be placed on the
scope of banks' activities. This question leads directly to consideration of
the most desirable or appropriate functions of different classes of financial
institutions. A study including review of this issue and its implications for
the country's financial structure has been commissioned by the Minister of
Finance. The report should be available by June 1985, and the Bank expects
its subsequent full discussion with MOF officials.



- 11 -
2.32      Steps to be taken:  A critical element in the liberalization of
commercial banks will be the development of their ability to earn revenues
sufficient to cover costs of administration, funds and risk, without
subsidies. Ultimately, liberalization and greater competition in finance
would create an environment where the banks could freely determine spreads in
relation to market conditions. This objective was not fully achieved in 1984
in spite of improvements in spreads permitted by the central bank and
permission to increase guarantee fees, which contribute significantly to bank
revenues. Bank profits have been diminished in the past by adverse rate
changes, which affect loans immediately but are applied only to new or renewed
rate bearing deposits. ProfIL levels for the five large city banks declined
from 0.7% of total assets in 1980 to 0.1% in 1983, but measures taken in 1984
have facilitated their recovery, and further improvement is expected as a
result of government policy and better bank management.
2.33      A problematic element in reported bank profits is allowance for bad
debts. This factor is especially difficult to assess because of Government's
role in directed credit and tax regulations. Bad and doubtful debts, in part,
are related to Government instructions to banks during the era in which banks
were partially owned and closely controlled by the Government. These are not
treated as nonperforming assets by banks or by the Office of Bank Supervision
and Examination on the grounds of implicit Government responsibility. Regula-
tions limit to 1% of outstanding loans the amount of income that can be
charged off to bad debt losses for tax purposes. This level is also used for
corporate accounting purposes by the banks. In addition, loans are treated as
current when interest is paid and 10 or 20% of principal due (depending upon
the client) is repaid. Noncurrent loans that are fully secured are often not
treated as arrears and security is rarely called. Hence, provisions tend to
equal 1% of outstanding loans, and loans classified as doubtful are quite
small in relation to the level that might be expected as a result of high
levels of corporate indebtedness and export experience during the recession of
1980-82.
2.34      Regularization of the banks' portfolio position arising from the
effects of directed credit could facilitate improvements in the banks' capital
through retained earnings and the issue of new shares. A stronger effective
capital position would facilitate a decrease in the government's intervention
in banking activities. The appraisal mission recommended that the portfolio
issue be addressed through an exchange of government bonds for doubtful assets
arising from directed credit. The Government's reaction to this recommenda-
tion is that banks' profits will be adequate to cover bad debt losses and to
improve their capital positions.
2.35      An especially important development that will increase competition
in banking is making domestic and foreign banks subject to the same controls
and privileges. Putting all banks on an equal footing internationalizes the
Korean banking industry and will end arrangements that favored one type of
bank over another. In 1985, foreign banks are being allowed access to central
bank rediscount facilities, while foreign currency swap arrangements that
shielded them from foreign exchange risk are being phased out. Limi-ations on
foreign bank competition for Won deposits will be phased out beginni..g in
1986, and foreign banks will become subject to selective credit controls,



- 12 -
primarily portfolio requirements favoring small and medium industry (para.
2.28). Restriction on domestic branching by foreign banks in Korea is also
expected to be relaxed.
2.36      Korean financial institutions, with Government and World Bank
support, are strengthening their training programs to enhance the capability
of their staff to operate in a new, more competitive environment. The Bank
has long been involved in training for KDB and KLB staff, and under the first
Industrial Finance Project these facilities were expanded to include commer-
cial bankers, central bankers and MOF staff. The technical assistance
component of the proposed project is designed to expand this activity (paras.
4.57-4.63).
Control of Corporate Indebtedness
2.37      Objective:  The Government's objective in dealing with corporate
indebtedness is to have debt capacity increasingly determined by market forces
(Annex 1, paras. 23-26; Annex 2, Section D). Its achievement requires the
careful structuring of measures that permit market forces gradually to exert
more influence on corporate financial behavior, while retaining measures to
ensure that excessive indebtedness of individual corporations does not lead to
crises.
2.38      Corporate indebtedness in Korea is relativeLy high by international
standards (para. 1.02), reflecting extensive use of debt to finance Korea's
remarkable economic growth. Success in export-led development has involved
competition in industries with relatively small margins which have not facili-
tated the accumulation of equity internally in the form of retained
earnings. Corporate indebtedness has posed problems in Korea when changes in
the international economic climate have diminished the corporate cash flow
available for debt service, as during the recession of 1980-82. High levels
of corporate indebtedness are difficult to address directly with financial
Liberalization: direct controls ultimately work against liberalization. But,
so long as corporate debt levels constitute a serious problem, direct
government action is likely. At the same time, however, indirect measures can
ameliorate the problem.
2.39      Progress towards this objective has included measures to help
corporations service debt, direct limitations on corporate debt and incentives
that would improve corporate capital structure. Measures to help corporations
to service debt have diminished as the effects of recession have passed. The
major actici.; of this type was the reduction of the commercial bank lending
rate to 10% in JuntY ;82, and simultaneous reductions in rates charged by
other regulated lenders. Direct limitations on debt have taken the form of
debt-to-equity ratios specified for major industries. These are applied to
restrict the type of corporate investment activity permitted when firms
exceeding these limits seek to increase their borrowing from institutional
lenders. In 1984, special scrutiny was directed toward the debt levels of
Large corporations, and restrictions were imposed on their borrowing from
foreign banks in Korea. Additional provisions for scrutiny and restrictions
have been introduced in 1985. An instrument for enforcing discipline on
corporate borrowing through the domestic banks is the prime bank agreement of



- 13 -
January 1984, which resembles a commercial bank term loan agreement and
enables the BOK to use commercial banks to control the indebtedness of large
borrowers according to government guidelines.
2.40      Incentives for corporations to reduce their indebtedness included
the increases in lending rates in 1984 and 1985. Numerous other measures have
made shareholding more attractive to potential equity investors and provided
incentives for corporations to sell shares to the public. One effect of the
promotion of equities as an alternative to debt is that corporations gain
increased access to funds provided as permanent capital, with no debt
servicing obligation. Such funds can be used to reduce debt directly or to
invest in assets that will generate cash flow that will permit debt to be
serviced more easily.
2.41      Steps to be taken for dealing with corporate indebtedness in the
near term include a continuation of direct measures applied to large corpora-
tions, continuation of limits on debt-to-equity relationships to control the
types of expansion permitted to firms seeking increased debt, accounting
reforms that will require the consolidation of the accounts of large corporate
groups, introduction of credit rating and information services through
independent agencies, efforts to decontrol interest rates, and fiscal and
other incentives for the promotion of equity investment. These measures, over
time, should help lending institutions to develop their credit criteria, shift
the control of corporate indebtedness to market mechanisms and lessen the
extent of government involvement, as stated in the Policy Letter in Annex 1.
Equity Market Development
2.42      Objective:  The Government's objective in promoting equity market
development is to make equity investment increasingly attractive to individual
investors, to strengthen the institutional framework for issuing and trading
equities, and to provide incentives for corporations to issue equity (Annex 1,
paras. 27-35; Annex 2, Section E).  A more dynamic equity market will help to
make risk-reward relationships more transparent, increasing the scope for
efficient investment.  It will also enable more entrepreneurs to obtain
finance, widen participation in the capital gains that should accompany growth
of the Korean economy, facilitate the development of a modern corporate
structure characterized by private ownership, and help to ease the problem of
corporate indebtedness by creating financial claims that do not have to be
repaid.
2.43      Progress in equity market development has been significant and broad
ranging. The Government has created institutions to ensure orderly trading on
the Korea Stock Exchange. This effort has been successful, resulting in more
than 10 years of trading without any major irregularities. Listing
requirements, procedures for settling trades, and facilities for safekeeping
of securities have been managed satisfactorily. The most remarkable
government measure has been the required listing of large, closely-held
firms. This has been aimed at successful companies and has been implemented
to spread wealth and ownership more widely, obtain disclosure of corporate
finances and create a larger pool of marketable securities. Reflecting this
effort and incentives for issuers, the stock of more than 330 firms is listed
on the Korea Stock Exchange.



- 14 -
2.44      Stock market development has been constrained by regulations that
have limited new issues by permitting flotation only at par value and by
government effort to influence the leveL of dividends. The Government no
longer attempts, formally or informally, to control dividend rates, and has
eased restrictions on new issues by permitting firms whose outstanding shares
trade at prices significantly above par value to issue additional shares at
market prices.
2.45      Efforts to broaden the investor base have included special fiscal
incentives for small investors. The use of fiscal incentives has also includ-
ed corporate tax credits for firms issuing shares. Listing requirements have
been eased, by reducing to 20% the proportion of equity required to be placed
in an initial public offering, in order to create a wider range of securi-
ties. The pool of investment funds has also been augmented by international-
ization of the market through the launching of three closed-end funds in
foreign markets. The largest of these is the $60 million Korea Fund, launched
on the New York Stock Exchange in 1984 with IFC assistance. Internationaliza-
tion has also included permitting foreign brokerage firms to obtain interests
of up to 10% in the equity of Korean brokerage houses, and the first of these
deals is expected to occur in 1985.
2.46      Steps to be taken to promote the development of equity markets are
expected to include greater use of fiscal incentives for shareholders and
issuers with due regard for their implication for government finances.
Regulatory changes to permit increased investment in equities by insurance
companies, pension funds and other financial firms, and by foreign portfolio
investors are expected by 1986, as are measures to encourage the formation of
pension funds. Measures are also planned that should lower investors'
transaction costs through the provision of better financial information about
listed firms, including requiring consolidated accounts for large groups of
related firms. Intercompany equity and debt holdings beyond conventional
trade credit will be subjected to increased disclosure and ultimately
discouraged by changes in tax laws. Insider transactions will be subjected to
more rigorous reporting requirements. A rating agency is being established to
introduce independent credit and investment ratings to Korea. Full liberal-
ization of issuing regulations to permit all listed firms to issue new shares
at market is expected. Government initiatives are also expected to expand
market access and the range of securities through revitalization of the over-
the-counter market.
Monitoring Financial Reform
2.47      The Bank has monitored Korea's success in financial sector reform
since it began in 1981. Under this project the Bank and the Government would
exchange views on progress achieved in carrying out the program of financial
sector reform, based on annual summaries of progress to be prepared by MOF and
periodic reviews of plans for additional reform measures. Particular
attention will be given to determining whether the very significant improve-
ments already achieved are maintained, and the extent of further liberaliza-
tion. Details of specific measures to be taken are contained in Annex 1 and
Annex 2.



- 15 -
II. THE INDUSTRIAL LENDING STRATEGY
Past Operations
3.01      The Bank's industrial lending strategy in Korea has focussed on
strengthening finaj5ial intermediaries lending long-term resources to indus-
trial enterprises.-   This emphasis began in 1968 with support for establish-
ment of the Korea Development Finance Corporation (KDFC), a predecessor of KLB
(para. 4.02), and was extended to the Korea Development Bank (KDB) in 1975.
Small firms have been assisted through Bank loans to the Small and Medium
Industry Bank (SMIB) since 1975 and the Citi gns National Bank (CNB) since
1980. The Bank has lent about $1.4 billion - to the industrial sector
through these institutions.
3.02      The objectives of Bank loans to Korean deveLopment finance
institutions have been: (a) provision of foreign exchange to support indus-
trial investment; (b) improved efficiency of financial intermediaries; and
(c) support of the Government's industrial investment priorities for export
promotion, regional development, employment generation and skill develop-
ment. Project Completion Reports (PCRs) for Loans 735-KO, 905-KO and 1145-KO
to KLB, for Loan 1095-KO to KDB and for Loan 1175-KO to SMIB indicate, in
general, that project implementation has been excellent and the Bank's
objectives have been achieved. The PCRs noted that Bank resources had been
efficiently allocated to industrial projects yielding substantial financial
and economic benefits and that Bank loans supported the Government's priori-
ties in the industrial sector. Project Performance Audit Reports tPPARs) for
Loans 735-KO, 905-KO, 1145-KO and 1095-KO confirm these conclusions and note
the high standard of KLB and KDB's operations and their developmental record.
Lending Strategy i the 1980s
3.03      In the late 1970s, the Government and the Bank placed greater
emphasis on industrial and financial policy. The Bank's lending strategy in
the industrial sector has three main dimensions: (a) structural adjustment
loans (SAL) which focus on the industrial sector, paying particular attention
to trade and incentive policies; (b) industrial finance projects which are
increasingly associated with broader financial policies; and (c) subsector
projects for specific industries to develop the country's technological capa-
bilities. SAL components promoting industrial efficiency focused primarily on
trade and investment policies. Subsector projects include loans to the Korea
1/   Two exceptions are the Korea Machinery Loan (Loan 1466-KO) and the
Electronics Development Project (Loan 1676-KO).
2/   Nine loans for $528.3 million have been made to KLB, five loans for
$482.5 million have gone to KDB; SMIB has received four loans for
$205 million and two loans for $60 million have been made to CUB.
Loan 2215-KO (SMI machinery) allocated $59 million; through SHIB
($29 million), KLB ($20 million) and CNB ($40 million).



- 16 -
Institutes of Electronics Technology (KIET), the Korea Technolog; Development
Corporation (KTDC) and the Small and Mediun Industry Promotion Corporation
(SMIPC). These finance the transfer of technology and upgrade skills so that
Korean industry remains competitive as its advantage shifts toward technology-
intensive production.
3.04      Korean development finance companies (DFCs) have become more mature
and rely less on Bank assistance. This has permitted the Bank to use DFC
lending to address broad financial sector objectives in addition to institu-
tional issues.  The Bank raised the issue of financial 1iberalization with the
Government as early as the 1977 Basic Economic Report.3  The Bank's Financial
Sector Review initiated in 1979 pursued this theme, but the case for reform
remained weak while the Korean economy remained vibrant. As the effects of
overinvestment in subsidized subsectors and of the economic recession became
manifest in 1979-80, and with the advent of a new economic management team,
the Government decided to reduce centralized economic decision-making and to
place greater reliance on market mechanisms. The Fifth Five-Year Plan (1982-
86) calls for decentralization of investment decisions in the real sector and
liberalization of resource mobilization and all cation in the financial
sector.  The first Industrial Finance Project -  supported financial sector
reform in 1983 and 1984; considerable progress has been made as described in
Chapter II and Annex 1. The proposed project would build from this base and
support further reform measures in 1985-87 as set out in Chapter II, in the
Government's Policy Letter in Annex 1, and in Annex 2. The proposed loan and
additional financial sector work would support the Bank's continued dialogue
with the Government.
IV. THE PROJECT
A. Objectives and Description
4.01      The project will implement Bank strategy in Korea through support
for financial reform, technical assistance and financing for industrial
projects. The project objectives are to: (a) support the Government's finan-
cial sector reforms, particularly liberalization measures, through 1987
(paras. 2.17-46); (b) support industrial investment by providing KLB and KDB
$120 million and $100 million, respectively, for lending for the foreign
exchange costs of investments in plant and machinery by their clients in the
industrial sector; (c) assist project institutions (KLB and KDB) to adapt to
the changing financial sector environment; and (d) support government efforts
to strengthen the financial system through a technical assistance component of
$2 million to provide training for key officials of financial institutions and
related Government agencies. The following sections B and C summarize the
3/   Report No. 1489-KO dated February 23, 1977.  Growth and Prospects of the
Korean Economy.
4/   Loan 2309-KO, approved June 9, 1983; Staff Appraisal Report No. 4435-KO,
dated May 20, 1983.



- 17 -
characteristics of the two financial institutions eligible for participation
in the credit component. Section D describes the technical assistance
component. Detailed information on project institutions and on sector issues
are available in the project file (Annex 7).
B. Korea Long-Term Credit Bank
Institutional Aspects
4.02      The Korea Long-Term Credit Bank is the only private development
finance institution in Korea which provides medium- and long-term foreign and
domestic currency financing. KLB was incorporated in 1967 as the Korea
Development Finance Corporation (KDFC), and became a long-term credit bank in
June 1980 under the Long-Term Credit Bank (LTCB) Act of 1979. KDFC operated
almost exclusively as a relender of long-term foreign currency funds. Under
the LTCB Act, KLB is authorized to issue long-term domestic currency
debentures and to accept deposits from its corporate borrowers - activities
prohibited to KDFC as a corporation under the General Commercial Code. With
its authority to mobilize domestic currency funds, KLB's activities expanded
rapidly. Total assets increased almost fourfold during 1980-82, from W 209
billion to W 804 billion. Since 1982, total assets have increased at an
average annual rate of 19%, to W 1.1 trillion, or $1.3 billion, as of December
31, 1984.
4.03      KLB has achieved an impressive record of growth, financial
performance and institutional development, but KLB remains relatively small:
its assets represent about 1% of Korea's financial system. Still, KLB is an
important source of long-term credit: at year-end 1983 KLB held 6.6Z of
equipment loans outstanding to the manufacturing sector.
4.04      Management, Organization and Staffing.  KLB's lending guidelines are
established in its Statement of Investment and Operational Policies (Annex 3,
Attachment 1). The direction and emphasis of its operations are stated in its
Development Strategy Statement (Annex 3, Attachment 2). Overall policies and
operational guidelines are provided by the Board of Directors while investment
decisions are delegated to the Executive Committee (Annex 3, Table 1),
consisting of its President assisted by three managing directors. With its
rapid growth, KLB's staff has increased from 163 (84 professionals) in 1979 to
323 (176 professionals) in 1984. KLB has three branches and four subbranches
plus an office in Hong Kong.
4.05      Project Appraisal and Supervision.  KLB's project appraisal is
conducted by twenty professional staff. Appraisal covers managerial, tech-
nical, financial, marketing and economic aspects. The FRR is calcuated for
new and expansion projects involving total financing in excess of US$1.0 mil-
lion equivalent, while the ERR is calculated for projects having foreign
exchange financing in excess of US$1.0 million equivalent. A technical sub-
office comprised of seven engineers reviews proposed projects and their imple-
mentation. KLB's appraisals conform to World Bank standards. Supervision
consists primarily of monitoring disbursements, confirming project completion
and reviewing financial reports periodically provided by subborrowers. More
intensive supervision is undertaken whenever financial reports or repayment
difficulties indicate problems.



- 18 -
4.06      Procurement and Disbursement Procedures.  KLB's procurement
procedures conform to government guidelines and Bank requirements, generally
following (a) limited competitive bidding, with at least three competitive
quotations provided by potential suppliers, or (b) the proprietary method,
where compatability with existing equipment dictates a specific supplier.
KLB's procurement procedures effectively encourage competition, and competi-
tion among prospective suppliers is keen in the well-developed Korean market.
KLB's disbursements are authorized in accordance with progress in project
implementation and against proper documentation.
Resource Allocation
4.07      Overall Operations.  KLB's term Lending and investment operations
for the period 1979-84 (detailed in Annex 3, Table 2) are summarized below:

Loans constitute the bulk of KLB's operations. Total annual approvals have
grown at an average annual rate of 39% since 1980. With access to local
currency resources through the issue of debentures, domestic currency loan
approvals gradually exceeded foreign currency loan approvals. Since 1980,
domestic currency lending has accounted for 53.6% of KLB's total term lending
operations. This compares to 6.1% for the period 1968-79.
4.08      Characteristics of KLB Lending.  The major characteristics of KLB
loans are provided in Annex 3, Tables 3 and 4. Manufacturing continues to
account for the largest share, reaching 75.3Z in 1984. However, diversifica-
tion has been maintained, with any one subsector rarely accounting for more
than 20Z of annual loan approvals. During 1980-84, 40.8% of KLB's total
lending has been for working capital. Since 1980, almost 54.0% of all loans



- 19 -
approved exceeded W 1.0 billion, as KLB's clientele are concentrated in
medium-to-large size industries.  The maturity structure of KLB loans has
shortened in recent years, reflecting the growing importance of debentures in
KLB's resource mobilization efforts (paras. 4.13 and 4.22) against which
domestic currency loans are matched. Since 1980, 97.2% of local currency
loans approved (53.6Z of all loans approved) had maturities of 1 to 5 years,
while 57.4% of foreign currency loans approved (26.8% of all loans approved)
had maturities of 9 years or more.
4.09      Investment Operations.  Equity investments account for a small and
declining proportion of KLB's total operations. Purchases of bonds and
convertible debentures have increased to 13.7% of total approvals since 1980.
As of December 31, 1984, KLB's outstanding equity and debenture portfolios
amounted to W 8.5 billion and W 120.9 billion, respectively (0.9% and 13.3%,
respectively, of KLB's total portfolio).
Economic Impact and Promotional Activities
4.10      Economic Impact.  Partial economic indicators for 1,036 projects
financed by KLB with official and commercial foreign currency resources since
1968 are saumarized in Annex 3, Table 6.  An ex-ante evaluation of these
projects shows a total project investment of W 2.0 trillion, of which W 0.9
trillion (40%) was financed by KLB. Annual incremental project sales were
slightly more than W 4.2 trillion, 46% of wuch were exports.  These projects
created 68,800 jobs.  Average cost-per-job -  steadily increased, from $23,672
in 1975-76 to $78,850 in 1982, but fell to $48,678 and $61,370 in 1983 and
1984, respectively. KLB's growing SMI and electronics subsector financing are
major factors in the shift towards more labor-intensive projects. As shown in
Annex 3, Table 7, about one quarter of total KLB loans approved are to SMI
enterprises.
4.11      Promotional Activities.  To increase the range of service to Korean
industries, KLB (in close collaboration with IFC) helped establish the Korea
Investment and Finance Corporation tKIFC), the Korea Development Leasing
Corporation (KDLC) and the Korea Technology Advancement Corporation (KTAC).
KLB is the major shareholder in KIFC and KDLC. Since 1981 KLB has established
a Business Promotion Center, a Loan and Investment Consulting Office, and a
Consulting Center for International Investment to serve clients, and has
undertaken major research studies for the Government, including industrializa-
tion prospects for Cheju Island and the Daegu Region.
4.12      Utilization of Bank Loans 1932-KO and 2309-KO.  The Bank's eighth
loan to KLB (Loan 1932-KO) for $90.0 million is fully committed and as of
March 31, 1985, $88.0 million was disbursed. Ex-ante evaluation of the 70
projects financed under Loan 1932-KO shows a total investment cost of $442.3
million equivalent (Annex 3, Table 8). Almost 5,600 new jobs are expected to
be created (600 more than projected at the time of appraisal), at an invest-
ment cost per job of $79,500. Financial rates of return ranged from 12.9% to

33.3% while economic rates of return ranged from 10.5Z to 48.6Z (similar to
expectations at appraisal).  The median FKR was 24Z and the median ERR was

KLB Financial Condition and Performance
4.13      Assets and Liabilities.  KLB's total assets grew at an average
annual rate of 402 during the period 1980-83. Since 1980, KLB's increased
reliance on domestic resources has significantly changed the composition of
its principal assets and liabilities. Its domestic currency portfolio 6/
increased from W 15.2 billion (7.3% of total assets) in 1979 to W 428.3 bil-
lion (40% of total assets) as of December 31, 1984. As the domestic debenture
market has a shorter time horizon (primarily 1 to 3 years) than KLB's foreign
creditors (from 7 to 15 years), KLB's lending increasingly reflects the local-
ization of its resource base. Prior to 1980, loans approved with maturities
exceeding five years accounted for 92% of all loans approved; those with
maturities exceeding nine years accounted for 61%. Since 1980, the respective
figures are 43% and 27%.
4.14      As of December 31, 1984, 83.9% of KLB's total assets were financed
with long-term liabilities, 8.8Z with current liabilities and 7.3% with
equity. Foreign currency borrowings, 99.0% of KLB's long-term liabilities in
1979, were 53.7Z as of December 31, 1984, reflecting KLB's increasing reliance
on the debenture market. As of December 31, 1984, outstanding debentures
totaled W 435.1 billion, or 46.1% of KLB's total long-term liabilities. KLB's
total net worth increased from W 64.4 billion in 1980 to W 82.7 as of December
31, 1984.
4.15      Debt-to-Equity Position.  As of December 31, 1984, KLB's long-term
debt-to-equity ratio was 10.8:1; in 1986 this ratio is expected to exceed the
12.0:i maximum covenanted under Loan 2309-KO. However, it is unlikely that
KLB will be able to increase its paid-in capital in the near future because of
Government regulations concerning new issues by companies whose shares trade
below (para. 2.44). KLB's shares currently trade at about 90% of par, even
though it has had an annual return on average equity of at least 11.0% in
recent years, and increased its reserves and retained earnings to 65% of paid-
in capital as of December 31, 1984 (compared to 28.7% in 1980). In view of
KLB's financial, managerial and operational performance, and the quality of
its loan portfolio and high collection rate (para. 4.18), KLB's long-term debt
6/   Term-loans, debentures purchased and equity investments.



- 21 -
to equity limit would be raised to 15.0:1. KLB's debt service and interest
coverage ratios are adequate, as is its current ratio (Annex 3, Tables 13 and
14).
4.16      Financial Performante.  After a severe decline in effective interest
spreads 71 from 3.3Z in 1980 to 0.27% in 1982, KLB showed significant improve-
ment in 1983 and 1984 with spreads of 0.64% and 1.06%, respectively. The
decline was largely attributable to KLB's loans made with debenture resources
resulting in negacive spreads. While lending rates on these loans fell by
10.2 percentage points, from 34.0% to 23.9%, between June 1980 and June 1982,
the weighted average cost of its debentures declined by only 6.3 percentage
points, from 30.5Z to 24.3%. At year-end 1982, three- and five-year deben-
tures issued by KLB in 1980 were outstanding at a cost of 27% to 30%, while
new debentures were being issued at a cost of 11.98Z to 14.62Z. In the
meantime, KLB's lending rate had been lowered for new loans and also for
existing balances outstanding. The lending rate decline was required to main-
tain KLB's competitiveness and to support the government objective of easing
the burden of debt on the corporate sector. As a consequence, KLB's weighted
average effective spread on loans funded with debentures declined from 3.52%
in June 1980 to 2.08Z in June 1981 and -0.37% in June 1982. As the earlier
and more expensive debenture issues were redeemed and interest rates stab-
ilized, the weighted average effective spread on loans funded with debentures
improved from 0.17% in June 1983 to 1.03Z in December 1984.
4.17      The improvement in interest spreads, along with management's success
in controlling administrative and general expenses, reversed KLB's two-year
decline in earnings: 1984 income before and after tax was W 12.2 billion and
W 9.5 billion, respectively (Annex 3, Table 11). Return on average equity in
1984 was 11.9%, reversing a four-year decline. The proposed increase in KuB's
debt-to-equity limit (para. 4.15) should contribute to further improvement in
KLB's return on equity.  Overall, KLB's performance has been satisfactory
during a period of considerable difficulty for much of the country's financial
sector.
