4.19      Audit.  KLB's accounts for 1983, audited according to international
standards by an independent auditor acceptable to the Bank, were certified
without qualification. The auditors stated that provisions were adequate.
Resource Mobilization
4.20      Overview.  KLB has been very successful in mobilizing domestic and
foreign currency resources, with its total resource base increasing at an
average annual rate of 38% over the past five years. Since KLB's conversion
into a long-term credit bank in 1980, foreign currency borrowings as a
percentage of total resources outstanding have steadily declined from 70.3% in
1980 to 48.0% as of December 31, 1984.
4.21      Foreign Currency Resource Mobilization.  Until its first syndicated
commercial borrowing in 1976 with IFC's assistance, KLB obtained foreign
currency funds only from official sources, primarily IBRD and ADB. Since
then, KLB has borrowed $414.8 million from commercial sources and $650.0 mil-
lion from official sources (Annex 3, Table 18). The percentage of KLB's com-
mercial borrowing to total annual foreign currency borrowing has fluctuated
widely, with a composite percentage for 1976-1984 of 39%. While commercial
borrowings are an increasingly important source of KLB's total foreign
currency resources, KLB relies heavily on foreign official borrowings to fund
its long maturity equipment loans.
4.22      Domestic Currency Resources.  KLB's domestic currency resource base
was initially very small (W 21.7 billion in 1979), comprised entirely of a
government loan and equity. Today, KLB relies primarily on debenture issues
to raise domestic funds. In 1984, debentures provided 79.3% of its total
domestic resources. Net proceeds from debenture issues increased almost
fivefold between 1980 and 1984 to W 193.5 billion (Annex 3, Table 19) and
account for more than 41% of KLB's total outstanding resources.
4.23      Operational and Financial Projections.  KLB projections indicate
that loan, debenture, and equity investment approvals will grow at an average
annual rate of about 14% during the period 1985-89; domestic currency
approvals would increase at a rate of 17% and foreign currency approvals by
about 9%. The average annual growth rate for 1980-83 was about 18% for total
approvals (28% for domestic currency and 8% for foreign currency), reflecting
the large and rapid expansion in operations which initially followed KLB's
conversion into a long-term credit bank in 1980. Of projected total approvals
amounting to $3.2 billion equivalent, 66% would be domestic currency opera-
tions and 34% in foreign currency loans.
4.24      As of December 31, 1984, KLB had $80.6 million available for foreign
currency approvals, compared to a projected foreign currency requirement of
$1.1 billion for 1985-89. Domestic currency resources available for approval
were W 78.6 billion while projected requirements total W 1.7 trillion (Annex
3, Table 21). KLB plans to mobilize W 2.8 trillion ($3.4 billion) over the
1985-89 period, of which 69% would be in domestic currency (debentures) and
31Z in foreign currency (Annex 3, Table 22). KLB plans to raise $500 million
in foreign currency from commercial sources and $540 million from foreign
official sources. The proposed Bank loan of $120 million would be used



- 23 -
primarily to finance longer maturity foreign currency loans. During the
period in which the Bank loan is expected to be committed, it would represent
about 29Z of KLB's projected foreign currency loan approvals.
4.25      KLB's net income, W 9.5 billion in 1984, is expected to increase to
W 10.8 billion in 1985 and to W 15.9 billion in 1989, with return on average
equity projected to range between 12Z and 14Z during 1985-89. Throughout the
projection period, KLB's debt-service coverage ratio would remain at about
1.1:1. The long-term debt to equity ratio of 15:1 proposed under this loan
(para. 4.15) would be adequate through 1989.
Future Role
4.26      KLB's greatest challenge is to compete in an increasingly libera-
lized financial system. KLB's difficulties in issuing debentures in late 1982
highlighted its dependence on a single type of instrument to raise domestic
funds. Moreover, the LTCB Act restricts KLB's credit operations, reducing its
flexibility compared to other financial institutions whose operations are
being gradually deregulated. In June 1984, KLB submitted a number of recom-
mendations to MOF which would permit KLB to diversify and expand its domestic
resource mobilization base and to provide additional services for its clients.
Most of these recommendations require amendment of the LTCB Act.  MOF has
suspended action on KLB's recommendations pending a review of liberalization
proposals currently under consideration. KLB's recommendations are generalLy
consistent with the Government's financial sector liberalization program.
C. The Korea Development Bank
Institutional Aspects
4.27      Legal Framework, Ownership and Lending Strategy.  KDB was
established in 1954 under the KDB Act. Its authorized share capital was
increased to W 1 trillion ($1.2 billion) in 1981. As of December 31, 1984,
KDB was the largest financial institution in Korea with total assets of W 8.1
trillion ($9.5 billion). KDB is also the largest source of term finance for
industry. As a Government-owned development bank, KDB operates primarily as
the financing arm of the Government's industrial investment policies. KDB's
lending to the private sector, however, increased from 12X of annual commit-
ments in 1978 to 72Z in 1983 and 1984. Much of this lending is to large firms
in industries encouraged by the Government.
4.28      Broad lending guidelines and objectives are stated in KDB's Policy
Statement (Annex 4, Attachment 1). As provided under "The Government-Invested
Enterprise Management Act and Employment Decree", a Board of Directors (Annex
4, Table 1) was established by KDB in May 1984. This Board provides overall
policy direction and guidance. Day-to-day operating policies are established
by KDB's Major Management Group, consisting of the Governor, Deputy Governor,
and seven Assistant Governors. Details of KDB's organizational structure are
provided in Annex 4, Chart 1. KDB's Development Strategy Statement (Annex 4,
Attachment 2) sets out the short-term strategy KDB would employ to achieve its
longer term objectives.



- 24 -
4.29      Organization, Management and Staffing.  KDB has twelve local
offices, seven representative offices and two subsidiaries overseas to assist
its foreign currency borrowing. As of December 31, 1984, KDB's staff numbered
1,915, of which 609 were professional staff. In mid-1984, KDB reorganized,
disbanding the Foreign Loan Department (the department previously responsible
for Bank loans) and strengthening the Planning Department. The objective of
the reorganization was to decentralize loan decision-making, place more
responsibility on Loan officers and integrate KDB's relationships with clients
in order to compete more effectively.
4.30      Project Appraisal and Supervision.  All project proposals are
appraised by KDB; Loans in excess of W 1 billion are given extensive financial
and economic analysis; financial and economic rates of return are calculated
for loans from official sources of $750,000 and above and for other projects
whose cost exceeds W 2 billion. KDB appraisal procedures for both Bank-
financed and other projects are thorough and include a cash flow analysis;
KDB's appraisals conform to World Bank standards. Project supervision chiefly
consists of reviewing the quarterly operational r egqrts and annual financial
statements that borrowers are required to submit.-    Site visits are used to
verify collateral, to confer with borrowers who might be experiencing market
downturns, and to check on problems noted in quarterly or annual reports.
4.31      Procurement and Disbursement.  KDB's procurement procedures are
sound and ensure a reasonable degree of competition. They conform to
Government guidelines and Bank requirements, and include limited competitive
bidding requiring at least three quotations, and proprietary contracts in
qualifying cases. KDB's limited competitive bidding procedures effectively
encourage competition, particularly as the Korean market is highly developed
and competition among potential suppliers is keen; in addition, offers
provided by suppliers are reviewed by KDB's Technical Service Department to
help ensure that prices are reasonable. KDB's disbursement procedures are
adequate and ensure that funds are made available in accordance with progress
in project implementation and supported with proper documentation.
Resource Allocation
4.32      Lending.  As of December 31, 1984, KDB's outstanding loan portfolio
amounted to W 5.9 trillion, or $6.9 billion (Annex 4, Table 2). Total loan
commitments increased at an average annual rate of 49% over the 1978-80
period. Growth fell to an average of slightly less than 14% through 1984,
althought a surge in loan commitments in the second half of 1984 contributed
significantly to this average. The slower growth of commitments through 1984
is in part attributable to the economic recession of the early 1980s. More
recently, the increasing competitiveness of other financial institutions, the
decline in directed credit programs, a scarcity of resources available for
lending at administered rates and government policy to limit lending to large,
heavily indebted firms have influenced KDB's performance. Foreign currency
10/ Borrowers listed on the Korea Stock Exchar3e are required to submit
audited annual financial statements.



- 25 -
loan commitments have accounted for about 13% of total 1980-84 loan
commitments.
4.33      In addition to supporting large-scale li/ manufacturing industries,
nearly 34Z of KDB's 1984 loan commitments were for major projects in the
transportation and power sectors. The sectoral distribution of loans has
remained relatively constant since 1980, the share of manufacturing industries
ranging from 47% in 1983 to 57% in 1980 and 1982 (Annex 4, Table 4). Working
capital loans have become increasingly important in KDB's portfolio, account-
ing for only 16% of loan commitments in 1978-79 compared to roughly 40Z over
the period 1980-84 (Annex 4, Table 3). This increase reflects the tight
liquidity situation and the corporate sector's efforts to utilize existing
production capacity idled by the recession.
4.34      Investments.  As of December 31, 1984, KDB's investment portfolio
amounted to W 816.5 billion ($961 million), of which 70% were equity invest-
ments and 30X were bonds and debentures (Annex 4, Table 5). Many of KDB's
equity holdings arise from Government transfers of shares in state sector
companies as contributions to KDB's equity; these holdings provide KDB with
relatively low returns. KDB's dividend income from its equity investments has
been steadily declining since 1980 and amounted to 0.8% of average equity
investments in 1983 and 1984. KDB has incorporated in its Development
Strategy Statement for 1985-90 principles governing the management of its
equity portfolio (para. 4.28). Since 1980, KDB's bond and debenture portfolio
has grown at an average annual rate of 54Z. It has-been substantially more
profitable than KDB's equity investments: interest income for 1984 was 11.4%
of average bond and debenture investments.
4.35      Guarantees.  KDB's outstanding guarantee portfolio declined over the
1980-84 period from W 4.8 trillion ($5.6 billion) in 1980 to W 3.6 trillion
($4.2 billion) in 1984 (Annex 4, Table 7). Almost 90% of KDB's outstanding
guarantees are in foreign currency, and 76Z are counterguaranteed by the
Government under a Guarantee Release Agreement (GRA) designed to promote
lending accorded priority by the Government.
4.36      Directed Lending.  Major government lending schemes for industry 12/
are at least in part administered by KDB. Government direction of KDB's
lending is significant, but the extent depends on the source of funding.  In
certain instances the Government uses KDB as a conduit to channel budget
appropriations to specific public sector entities. This type of lending
increased slightly over the 1980-84 period, from 12.2Z to 15.9% of KDB's
outstanding loan portfolio (Annex 4, Table 9). KDB is also administrator of
ll/ Enterprilses employing more than 300 persons and with total assets exceed-
ing W 500 million.
12/ These include the National Investment Fund (NIF), Tourist Development
Fund, Energy Rationalization Fund, Industrial Rationalization Fund,
Shipping Promotion Fund, Special Industry Supporting Funds and Electronic
Industry Promotion Fund.



- 26 -
some selective lending schemes designed to promote specific industries, and
determines the eligibility of individual firms under broad sectoral guide-
lines. During the period 1980-83, these programs represented 34% to 37% of
KDB's total loans outstanding; in 1984 the corresponding figure was only
23%. Government funding throuigh budget allocations and special funds provided
resources for slightly less than 50% of KDB's outstanding portfolio over the
1980-83 period, and 39% in 1984. KDB is responsible for financing the
remainder with other resources. KDB has discretion in the allocation of funds
mobilized through deposits, debentures and foreign borrowings, subject to the
KDB Act.
Developmental Impact
4.37      Economic Impact.  The 4,609 projects financed by KDB from 1978
through 1983 had a total investment cost of W 13.2 trillion, of which W 7.3
trillion (55%) was financed by KDB (Annex 4, Table 10). Incremental employ-
ment from these investments was expected to exceed 600,000 and annual incre-
mental sales were estimated at W 23.0 trillion, 42Z of which represents
exports.
4.38      Utilization of Bank Loans 1933-KO and 2309-KO.  As of year-end 1984,
KDB had committed $99.9 million out of $100 million available under Loan 1933-
KO; disbursements totaled $99.8 million. The 66 subprojects financed had an
investment cost of $446.2 million equivalent ex-ante, 38.7% of which was
financed by KDB (Annex 4, Table 11). Subloan size ranged from $50,000 to
$10.0 million. The subprojects are expected to generate $155.8 million in
incremental annual exports and to employ almost 11,300 at an investment cost
per job of $39,600. Financial rates of return range from 13% to 38% while
economic rates of return range from 16% to 51%, with the median FRR and ERR
being 22Z and 26%, respectively. Under Loan 2309-KO, commitments as of March
31, 1985, totaled $59.2 million under KDB's $130.0 million credit component.
The 23 projects approved have an estimated total investment cost of $983.0
million equivalent and ar  ixpected to create some 2,050 jobs at an investment
cost-per-job of $479,200.3   The median FRR is 22%; the median ERR is 30%.
Details of the subprojects financed by KDB under Loan 2309-KO are provided in
Annex 4, Table 12.
Financial Condition and Performance
4.39      Financial Condition.  Over the 1980-84 period, KDB's assets grew at
an average annual rate of about 20%. As of December 31, 1984, 85% of KDB's
total assets of W 8.1 trillion were financed with long-term liabilities, 4%
with current liabilities and 11% with equity. At year-end 1984, KDB's current
ratio was 1.8:1. KDB received a W 45 billion contribution to paid-in capital
in 1984, bringing total paid-in capital to W 583.9 billion; total net worth
has gradually increased from W 633.8 billion in 1980 to W 870.2 billion as of
13/ Excluding one subproject (a refinery) with an investment cost of
$634 million creating 500 incremental jobs, the investment cost-per-job
is substantially smaller - $225,160.



- 27 -
December 31, 1984. KDB's long-term debt-to-equity ratio of 7.7:1 at year-end
1984 was within the 10:1 limit agreed previously with the Bank, and the debt
service coverage ratio remained satisfactory at 1.4 times.
4.40      Financial Performance.  The major issue facing KDB is its continuing
decline in profitability. This issue is directly linked to the choices KDB
faces in determining its role in a liberalized environment (para. 4.56). Net
profit as a percent of average total assets has steadily declined from 1.2% in
1980, to 0.9% in 1981, to 0.7% in 1982 and to 0.3Z in 1983; as a percentage of
average net worth, net profit decLined from 7.4% in 1980 to 2.5X in 1983
(Annex 4, Table 16). In 1984, this percentage improved to 4.4Z. However,
almost half of KDB's 1984 net profit is attributed to a net gain realized on
the sale of its previous main office building. Excluding this gain, KDB's
1984 adjusted return on average net worth was 2.5%. These returns have
generally been lower than the rate of inflation. KDB's low profitability is
attributable to loai spreads on its lending, the overhang of high cost deben-
ture issues and meager dividend income on its equity investments (para. 4.34).
4.41      KDB's gross operating spread declined from 2.5Z of average total
assets in 1980 to 1.0% in 1983, improving to 1.4% in 1984 (for the reason
noted in para. 4.40). While KDB has maintained the minimum 22 weighted
average effective spread on foreign commercial borrowings required under Loan
2309-KO, it receives a spread of only 0.5 to 1.5% on loans made under the
Government's special financing schemes. For the very limited amount of
lending funded through deposit resources, KDB's spread is from 2.0Z to 2.5%.
Adoption of a lending rate based on the effective cost of KDB's industrial
finance debenture (IFDs) plus an adequate spread would help improve KDB's
spreads on domestic currency lending supported by IFDs (para. 4.56). Special
Government financing schemes on projects financed through budgetary alloca-
tions account for almost 40% of KDB's portfolio. The spread on this lending
is expected primarily to cover administrative costs.
4.42      Proceeds from KDB's new IFDs are used to redeem IFD obligations and
to purchase corporate debentures. In the recent past, KDB made loans from the
proceeds of IFDs issued at a cost as high as 24%. When the 1982 lending rate
reduction to 10% was applied to outstanding as well as new loans, KDB incurred
high negative spreads on IFD-funded loans.  High cost issues are now being
redeemed by new IFD issues having a weighted average cost of about 13%, and it
is expected that KDB's losses on this operation should decline substantially
in 1985.
4.43      KDB's management has taken steps to address the decline in
profitability. In 1983, it established an Assets and Liabilities Management
Committee (ALCO), comprising the Deputy Governor, one Assistant Governor and
six General Managers. ALCO is responsible for generating recownendations
concerning assets and liabilities management and interest rate policy; the
proposal for a long-term base rate (para. 4.56) was one of its first major
recommendations. Other measures to improve profits recently adopted by KDB
include entering the call loan market, and offering overdraft and bill
discounting facilities. KDB is also placing greater emphasis on issuing
guarantees, particularly for corporate bonds.



- 28 -
4.44      To address KDB's profitability, it was agreed under the proposed
project to maintain the 2% weighted average effective spread requirement on
lending funded by foreign commercial borrowings. In addition, KDB has
reaffirmed in its Development Strategy Statement the objective of earning a
positive real yield on its equity. The adequacy of KDB's spreads on lending
from Government resources was discussed at negotiations. To make KDB commer-
cially viable, this spread would need to be significantly increased. KDB has
proposed to Government the adoption of the LTBR for this purpose (para.
4.56). In addition, it was agreed at negotiations that KDB would maintain an
interest rate structure that will enable it to earn a reasonable yield on its
equity.
4.45      Portfolio Quality.  The quality of KDB's guarantees appears to be
good. Although its equity portfolio does not contribute substantially to
profits, most of the investments' estimated market values exceed their cost.
Where this is not the case, KDB has made appropriate allowances for possible
losses. KDB's loan collection rate for both 1983 and 1984 was 98.5% (Annex 4,
Table 18). However, arrears have increased and as of December 31, 1984,
amounted to W 35.6 billion (0.6% of the outstanding portfolio), compared to
W 18.7 billion at year-end 1983 (0.3% of the outstanding portfolio).
Principal affected by arrears increased from W 147.8 billion (2.81 of out-
standing portfolio) to W 200.0 billion (3.4Z of outstanding portfolio) over
the same period. Reschedulings as a percentage of the outstanding portfolio,
which ranged from 4.6% to 5.7Z over the 1980-83 period, increased slightly to
6.3% in 1984.
4.46      While the increase in arrears is significant, 45% are for less than
three months, 23% are for 3 to 6 months, and none are in excess of two
years. Arrears are concentrated in the transportation sector, particularly
the marine transport industry which is experiencing world-wide overcapacity.
KDB and the Government are encouraging shipping company mergers, and
refinancing and rescheduling projects which are expected to be viable over the
long-term.
4.47      Audit.  KDB's accounts for 1984, audited according to international
standards by an independent auditor acceptable to the Bank, were certified
without qualification. The auditors stated that provisions were adequate.
Resource Mobilization
4.48      As of December 31, 1984, KDB's total resources amounted to
W 7.6 trillion ($8.9 billion) of which 60Z had been mobilized in domestic
currency (Annex 4, Table 20). On the asset side, 88% of KDB's loan portfolio
outstanding was in domestic currency. To meet the demand for domestic
currency loans, KDB converts some foreign exchange resources to domestic
currency.
4.49      Domestic Currency.  During 1980-84, a net total of W 4.8 trillion
was mobilized by KDB, of which 54% was in domestic currency.141 KDB's

principal sources of domestic currency are budget allocations and special
financing schemes which together represent about 60% of the net increase in
KDB's domestic currency resources outstanding. Relatively modest Government
contributions to equity and increases in reserves account for 14% of KDB's net
increase in domestic resources, while 26% was mobilized in the market in the
form of IFDs and deposits (Annex 4, Table 21).
4.50      KDB's direct mobilization of funds in the domestic market ranged
from 11% to 20X of incremental resources in 1980-83. In 1984, however,
W 235.0 billion, or 29.0% of the W 809.6 billion in total incremental
resources, was attributable to the sale of KDB's debentures. Fluctuations in
debenture issues reflect, in part, KDB's reliance on fixed rate debentures,
which were not attractive to investors in periods of inflation. KDB now also
offers floating rate coupon debentures with maturities of 3, 5 and 7 years.
4.51      Foreign Currency.  KDB issues foreign currency debentures through
private and public placements, undertakes syndicated commercial borrowings,
and taps foreign official loan sources, principally from ADB and IBRD. (Terms
and conditions of recent borrowings are given in Annex 4, Table 24.) After
Government funding, syndicated commercial borrowings are the largest source of
finance for KDB, funding almost 42% of KDB's loan portfolio. As of December
31, 1984, outstanding commercial borrowings amounted to W 2.4 trillion (US$2.8
billion). KDB's effective lending rate on Won funds obtained by converting
these foreign currency resources is in line with prevailing market rates. As
of December 31, 1984, this rate was 15.1%.
4.52      KDB's success in mobilizing foreign commercial borrowings at very
favorable terms has aLlowed a gradual reduction in its use of foreign official
funds, which accounted for only 8% of its total foreign borrowings in 1983 and
1984. However, KDB will continue to seek loans from foreign official sources,
as their long maturities facilitate financing of long-gestation projects.
4.53      Projections and Financing.  During the period 1985-89, loan
approvals are projected to increase at a modest rate of 6X per annum. This
slower growth in the volume of loan operations reflects both the likely
decline in loan demand by some of KDB's major clients as their ability to
borrow directly from the domestic and international capital market increases,
and a reduction in Government funding for special financial schemes
administered by KDB.
4.54      As of December 31, 1984, KDB's uncommitted long-term resources
amounted to W 143.4 billion ($168.7 million). To meet its projected
commitments, KDB plans to borrow approximately W 9.9 trillion ($12.1 billion)
during the period 1985-89 (Annex 4, Table 26). Domestic currency resources,
comprising government funds and debenture proceeds, would account for 63% of
total resources mobilized during the five-year period. Of these long-term
domestic currency resources, almost 59X would be derived from debenture
sales. Foreign currency borrowings during 1985-89 are projected at $4.5



- 30 -
billion, with about 9Z ($410 million) from official sources and the balance
from the international capital market. KDB's share of foreign currency
borrowings as a percentage of total borrowings would decline slightly, from
39% for 1982-84 to 37Z for 1985-89. During the period in which the Bank loan
is expected to be committed, it would represent less than 6% of total foreign
exchange resources borrowed.
4.55      After a continued slight decline in 1985, KDB's net profit is
expected to improve modestly, reaching 5% of average net worth by 1989. Over
the projection period, KDB's debt service ratio would remain between 1.1 and
1.3, which is satisfactory. The long-term debt-to-equity ratio is expected to
remain within the contractual limit of 10.1 through 1989.
4.56      Future Role.  KDB has reviewed its prospects based on reduced
government intervention in private economic activities and preferential credit
for which KDB is the principal agent. KDB management believes that as reforms
are implemented, directed lending by commercial and other banks would be
eliminated and KDB would increasingly specialize in making policy loans
designed to support Government investment priorities. Expected reductions in
government funding of directed credit would require KDB to tap market sources
to fund policy lending. To safeguard its profitability on policy lending, KDB
has proposed to MOF the establishment of a domestic currency LTBR based on the
cost of IFDs issued by KDB plus an adequate spread. The LTBR would be vari-
able and adjusted quarterly. The Bank supports KDB's proposal to base its
domestic currency lending rate on the cost of its funds. MOF has responded
sympathetically to the LTBR proposal and has expressed a willingness to
consider it at a later date. KDB has been requested to review its prospects
in a liberalized financial system and to amend its Development Strategy State-
ment to reflect any change in directiou that may be warranted (para. 4.28).
D. Technical Assistance
4.57      Financial reform implies changes in the skills required by MOF staff
responsible for financial sector policy. Liberalization and increased compe-
tition in commercial banking also would make new demands on commercial bankers
and bank supervisors which could be met irn part through training. The
technical assistance (TA) component of the project would support a training
program designed to meet these challenges and to expand upon the training
initiatives that began under the first Industrial Finance Project.
4.58      Specificaily, the technical assistance component of the project
would include three activities:
(a) assisting the training activities of the Korea Banking Institute
(KBI) through:
(i) development of training materials and training evaluation
through cooperation with the Bank's Economic Development
Institute (EDI). EDI would provide sample case study materials
to KBI and assign staff to work with KBI officials in
developing cases for use in Korea. Likewise, EDI staff would



- 31 -
assist KBI in its development of meins of monitoring training
course performance; and
(ii)  secondment of KBI officials to training institutions abroad for
short-term training and for longer-term on-the-job training;
(b) Lraining of Korean commercial bankers by:
(i)  bringing specialists to Korea under an expert invitation
program to conduct two-week senior seminars at KBI on credit
analysis and loan practice, foreign exchange management and
international banking, commercial bank management, and internal
audit and control.  Four hundred Korean commercial bankers are
expected to participate in these senior seminars; and
(ii) sending Korean bankers abroad for seminars at commercial bank
training centers and universities, and on-the-job training in
commercial banks. These activities would train about 100
Korean senior commercial bankers on topics of special interest
to those participating;
(c) overseas training of approximately 60 MOF officials at:
i?  institutions of higher learning, where staff from the MOF
bureaus responsible for financial sector policy would be
trained in economics and finance at the Masters degree level;
(ii) government institutions managing and regulating financial
systems in advanced countries, where Korean officials would
receive on-the-job training for periods of 3 to 6 months and
other shorter-term training. This component would be arranged
by HOF; and
(iii) specialized seminars on economics, financial mangement and
capital market development suitable for higher level staff.
4.59      Administration.  The training component elements involving
assistance to KBI and the training of commmnercial bankers would be coordinated
and supervised by KBI. KBI was established in 1976 by 11 banks to develop and
offer training programs for the staff of Korean financial institutions. The
ownership structure of KBI was changed in 1984, bringing it under the juris-
diction of the Korea Federation of Banks, when the BOK discontinued its mem-
bership as part of the Government's policy to limit its role in the affairs of
the commercial banks. These changes are not expected to diminish KBI's capa-
bility in coordinating the training program.
4.60      KBI is experienced in Bank procedures as it administers the training
program under the first Industrial Finance Project and has prepared a post-
implementation evaluation report, with the assistance of EDI, to evaluate the
seminars supported under the project. The post-implementation report was
provided to the Bank at the end of April 1985. The post-implementation report
contains useful recommendations relating to the selection and pre-departure



- 32 -
orientation of participants, course content and pedagogy, as well as to the
proper utilization of the skills acquired. After a full review of the report
within the Bank, the recommendations of the report would be discussed with the
Government and KBI and incorporated, as appropriate, in the training program
to be supported under the proposed project. The Coordinating Board set up in
connection with the first project, consisting of representatives of the
training managers of the commercial banks, would review the eligibility
criteria for participants under the proposed project, and modify them if
necessary in light of the findings of the post-implementation study.
4.61      KBI has provided the Bank a list of prospective training programs
and institutions abroad and annual training budgets and program plans based on
consultation with these institutions and on training requests of the partici-
pating commercial banks. KBI has agreed to provide the Bank for review by
December 31, 1985, the training program to be implemented during calendar year
1986, and not later than September 30 each year thereafter, the training
program to be implemented in subsequent years. The persons trained in Korea
and abroad and the foreign specialists brought to Korea under this element of
the training component would be selected according to criteria satisfactory to
the Bank. KBI would propose these criteria to the Bank by December 31, 1985.
4.62      The cost of the training program involving KBI and commercial
Nankers would be $1.0 million, which would be divided among the commercial
banks sending participants to these training activities. The costs of the
expert invitation program and the secondment of KBI personnel to institutions
abroad would be borne by the participating institutions on a pro rata basis.
The costs associated with sending Korean bankers abroad for training would be
borne by each bank on the basis of the actual expenses of -he staff member
sent abroad.
4.63      The administration of the training activities for Ministry of
Finance officials would be handled by MOF, following procedures established
under the first Industrial Finance Project. Officials to t-e trained would be
selected according to criteria satisfactory to the Bank. The overall cost of
$1.0 million for this element would be borne by the MOF on _he basis of the
actual expenses incurred for their respective officials trained abroad.
V. THE PROPOSED BANK LOAN
Features of the Proposed Loan
5.01      Relending Arrangements.  The proposed Bank loan of $222 million
would be made to the Government at the Bank's standard variable interest
rate. The Government would relend $120 million of the proceeds of the credit
component to KLB and $100 million to KDB on the same terms as the Bank's loan,
but with an administration fee of 0.05Z. The signing of subsidiary loan
agreements, satisfactory to the Bank, between the Government and KLB and KDB
would be a condition of effectiveness.
5.02      Under the $2 million technical assistance component, KBI's responsi-
bilities would be specified in an agency agreement to be entered into with the



- 33 -
Government. The signing of this agreement would be a condition of disburse-
ment for this component. The cost of the training of Ministry of Finance
officials, estimated at $1 million, would be borne by the Government. The
estimated $1 million cost of the expert invitation program, training of XBI
staff abroad and the training program for staff of commercial banks would be
borne by the participating banks (para. 4.62). The Government would make
loans available to these banks for this purpose. The loans would bear the
same interest rate as the Bank loan, with a maximum repayment period of
15 years including a maximum grace period of 3 years. The Government would
enter into a Standard Relending Agreement, acceptable to the Bank, with each
participating institution for the training of its staff under the project.
Bank approval of the Standard Relending Agreement would also be a condition of
disbursement for this component of the project.
5.03      Relending Terms.  KLB and KDB would relend the credit cemponent at a
variable interest rate at least 2% above the effective borrowing cost inclu-
sive of the Government 0.05% administrative fee, which is consistent with KLB
and KDB practice when onlending foreign official borrowings and is adequate to
cover the administrative costs involved. Based on the current Bank lending
rate of 9.29Z, the final rate to subborrowers would be about 11.5%, which is
highly positive in real terms (para. 2.22). Under the credit component, the
foreign exchange risk would be carried by the final subborrowers. For the
technical assistance component, the foreign exchange risk would be borne by
the Government for the training of its officials and by participating
institutions utilizing project funds for the training of their staffs.
5.04      Amortization Schedule.  The technical assistance component would
have a fixed amortization schedule and would be repaid by the Government in
accordance with the standard terms applicable to Korea, i.e., a 15 year
maturity including a 3 year grace period. The credit component would have two
separate adjustable composite amortization schedules, one for KLB and one for
KDB, reflecting the aggregate amortization schedules of the subloans made by
each institution. Individual subloans would have a maximum maturity of 13
years, including a normal grace period of up to three years. As in previous
loans and to minimize substitution of Bank funds for local sources, KLB and
KDB are encouraged to cofinance with commercial sources. The Bank would
permit the use of early principal repayments on cof:nanced subloans to
amortize the commercial portion, and permit a grace period of up to five
years, with no change in the maximum maturity of 13 years.
5.05      Procurement.  Procurement under the credit component would be on the
basis of the standard procurement procedures of KDB and KLB as described in
paras. 4.06 and 4.31. Both institutions usually require their borrowers to
obtain at least three price quotations and competition among prospective
suppliers, both for local and imported goods, is keen. These procedures have
been reviewed and found to ensure satisfactory competition while minimizing
delays in procurement. KBI will be responsible for obtaining foreign specia-
lists under the expert invitation program following criteria and procedures
acceptable to the Bank. It will also be responsible for selecting institu-
tions abroad at which its own officials may be trained under the TA component.
Commercial banks will be encouraged to select institutions abroad for the
training of their staffs, and KBI will be expected to offer guidance in this



- 34 -
selection if requested to do so by any participating banks. All training
contracts would be subject to ex-post review, and contracts in excess of
$75,000 would also be subject to prior approval of the Bank.
5.06      Disbursements.  Special Accounts in the amount for $15.8 million for
KLB and $13.2 million for KDB will be established from which reimbursement of
subloans would be obtained. Disbursements would be made against full
documentation for subloans in excess of $2.0 million. For subloans of
$2.0 million or less, Statements of Expenditure (SOE) would be used. All
related supporting documents for SOEs, including evidence of payment, would be
retained by KLB and KDB and made available for review by Bank supervision
missions. As with previous Bank loans to KLB and KDB, the proposed loan would
finance: (a) 100% of the foreign exchange cost of goods and services imported
directly by subborrowers; and (b) 60% of the cost in domestic currency
(representing the estimated foreign exchange component) of locally manu-
factured or locally procured imported goods and equipment.
5.07      A special account of $200,000 for the TA component would be estab-
lished from which reimbursement of expenditures would be made. Disbursements
would be made against 100% of the cost of (a) overseas training for Ministry
staff, (b) overseas seminars and on-the-job training abroad for commercial
bank and KBI officials and (c) the expert invitation program. Expenditures
would be reimbursed on the basis of SOEs in cases where the amount involved is
less than $25,000. Supporting documents, including copies of contracts and
evidence of payment, would be retained in a central location, and made avail-
able for review by Bank supervision missions, and SOEs would be audited
annually by independent auditors acceptable to the Bank. For expenditures of
$25,000 or more, payments would be made directly to the institutions
concerned. The credit component of the loan would be comitted in two years
and disbursed in five years (Annex 6). This schedule conforms to the standard
Industrial Development Finance disbursement profile. The TA component would
also be disbursed in five years.
5.08      Eligibility Criteria.  Subloans appraised by KLB and KDB for medium-
and large-scale industrial projects (mainly in manufacturing, construction,
mining, transportation and utilities) would be eligible for financing under
the loan, subject to the firms' having sound financial structures. Under the
first Industrial Finance Project the maximum debt-to-equity ratio for eligible
subborrowers was reduced from 5:1 to 4.5:1 in an effort to improve their
financial structure by encouraging project sponsors to increase their equity
contributions. The 4.5:1 limit reflected the desire of KLB and KDB to have a
transitional period prior to introducing a more restrictive debt-to-equity
ceiling. The 4.5:1 limit has been retained under the project, as efforts
continue to manage corporate indebtedness in accordance with Government
policy.
5.09      Subloan Ceiling and Free Limit.  The free limit for subloans above
which KLB and KDB subproject appraisals are reviewed in detail by the Bank
prior to approval, would be increased from $5 million, as under the last two
lending operations, to $6 million. (This limit includes any outstanding
balances from previous loans used to finance the same investment.) Under the
two previous loans, as of March 31, 1985, the $5 million limit allowed review



- 35 -
of lOZ of KDB's subprojects representing 41% of the total amount approved,
while KLB has submitted four subprojects (14X of the total amount approved)
above the free limit. In addition, several of KLB and KDB's free-limit
subprojects have been spot-checked to ensure appraisal satisfactory to the
Bank.  The maximum subloan available to subborrowers under the loan would be
increa_.ed from $10 to $12 million, and KLB and KDB would not provide a total
of more than $15 million each to any single subborrower out of project funds.
5.10      Accounts and Audits.  KLB and KDB's accounts are audited annually by
independent auditors acceptable to the Bank. Future audits of these institu-
tions would include the Special Accounts and SOEs submitted under the project.
The project accounts (including SOEs) relating to the technical assistance
component would be audited by independent auditors acceptable to the Bank.
Audit reports would be submitted to the Bank within six months of the close of
the fiscal year.
Project Benefits and Risks
5.11      Benefits.  The project is designed to (a) promote financial sector
reform in Korea; (b) support financially and economically viable industrial
projects; (c) assist project institutions to adapt to a changing financial
sector ernvironment; and (d) improve institutional capabilities and supervisory
skills in the financial system through technical assistance and training.
5.12      The Bank has had an effective dialogue with the Government on finan-
cial sector issues since 1979, which has supported financial reform. The
Government has made considerable progress in gradually liberalizing the
financial system and the proposed loan would support the further steps to be
taken in the 1985-87 period. The new measures to be adopted through 1987,
which are spelled out in the Government's Policy Letter (Annex 1), would be
monitored closely during project supervision. These measures are expected to
contribute to improved resource mobilization and allocation, the development
of a stronger commercial banking system and revitalization of the equity
market. The policy dialogue would be supported by further financial sector
work by the Bank.
5.13      The $220 million credit component would support qualified investment
projects selected by KLB and KDB. Experience gained through previous lending
to these two institutions shows that the subprojects financed with Bank
resources have yielded substantial economic and financial benefits (paras.
4.10-4.12 and 4.37-4.38). It is expected that about 150 subprojects would be
financed under the credit component and that the financial and economic
benefits of these subprojects would be similar to those generated under
previous loans.
5.14      The institution-building impact of the proposed loan would support
the development of KLB and KDB through discussions of the future roles of
these institutions in a changing financial environment. These discussions
would continue to focus on their Policy Statements and Development Strategy
Statements as vehicles for definition of roles and strategies. Institution-
building efforts would also be directed toward the agencies participating in
the TA component through the training of key government and bank staff. KBI,



- 36 -
in particular, is expected to benefit from TA that would enable it to devote
more resources to developing training materials geared to the increasingly
competitive financial environment in Korea.
5.15      Risks.  Given KLB and KDB's record in administering previous Bank
loans, the institutionaL risks involved in the proposed project are minimal.
The institutional arrangements for the TA component will require careful
supervision because of their innovative nature and the number of institutions
that are expected to participate.
5.16      The risks facing Korean enterprises from their dependence on exports
are material and have been managed well in the past. Movement towards
liberalization, however, imposes transitional risks. These risks can be
contained by sensitivity on the part of subborrowers and prospective subbor-
rowers, KLB, KDB and the Government to changes in Korea's competitive
advantage, and to means by which technology transfer can be accelerated while
the risk of rising protectionism in markets abroad can be minimized. The risk
of diminished export competitiveness is minimized by the fact that sectors
affected are well-known to lenders. The danger of rising protectionism in
importing countries is mitigated by Korea's expanding range of trading part-
ners and product lines, and by arrangements for trade negotiations. The
recession of 1980-1982 had a negligible impact on KLB and KDB's reported
portfolio performance. This level of risk is not expected to be experienced
again for some time.
5.17      The main risk associated with the proposed loan relates to the
program of financial reform outlined in the Government's Policy Letter. WhiLe
commitment to the program is strong, changing domestic and international
economic and financial conditions might delay or weaken its implementation.
Since 1983, however, the Government has attached high priority to financial
sector reform and demonstrated that its cautious and consistent approach is
reasonable and likely to succeed.
VI. AGREEMENTS AND UNDERSTANDINGS REACHED AT NEGOTIATIONS
6.01      During loan negotiations the following agreements and understandings
were reached with the Korean Government:
(a) Condition of Board Presentation: The Government would provide to
the Bank its "Letter of Development Policy for the Financial Sector"
which would outline the major elements of reform and the steps
proposed for their implementation (para. 2.17).
(b) Condition of Loan Effectiveness: The signature of two subsidiary
loan agreements between the Government and KLB and KDB under terms
and conditions satisfactory to the Bank (para. 5.01).



- 37 -
(c) Condition of Disbursement: The signature of an agency agreement
between KBI and the Government which would specify KBI's responsi-
bilities would be a condition of disbursement for the training
component of the proposed loan. Approval by the Bank of a Standard
Relending Agreement' between the Government and institutions partici-
pating in the training component would also be a condition of dis-
bursement for this component (para. 5.02).
(d) Other Agreements
Ci)   The Bank and the Government would exchange views on progress
achieved in carrying out the program of financial sector reform
based on an annual summary of progress to be prepared by MOF
(para. 2.47).
(ii)   KDB would maintain an interest rate structure that will enable
it to earn a reasocable yield on its equity (para. 4.44).
(iii)   The Government would ensure that KBI would, by December 31,
1985, propose for Bank approval, the criteria to be used in
selecting participants and foreign specialists for the training
program (para. 4.61).
(iv)   The Government would ensure that MOF and KBI annually submit to
the Bank for approval the content, organization and cost of
their proposed training programs; in addition, any training
contract in excess of $75,000 would be subject to prior Bank
approval (paras. 4.61, 4.63 and 5.05).
Ce) Other Understandings
-i)   The study of Korea's financial structure commissioned by MOF
would be fully discussed with the Bank upon its completion
(para. 2.31).
6.02      During negotiations, the Bank reached the following agreements with
KLB and KDB:
(a) KLB's long-term debt-to-equity ratio limit would be increased to
15:1 (para. 4.15).
(b) KDB's long-term debt-to-equity ratio limit would be maintained at
10:1 (para. 4.39).
(c) KDB would maintain an average effective spread of at least 2Z when
onlending the proceeds of foreign currency resources mobilized from
commercial sources (para. 4.44).
(d) KLB and KDB would relend the proceeds of the credit component at a
variable interest rate of at least 2% above the effective borrowing
cost of Bank funds (pars. 5.03).



- 38 -
(e)  The marimum allowable debt-to-equity ratio for KLB and KDB sub-
borrowers under the proposed loan would be retained at 4.5:1
(para. 5.08).
(f)  The free limit on subloans, including outstanding balances financed
under previous Bank loans for the same investment, would be raised
to $6 million. The maximum subloan available under the loan to sub-
borrowers would be $12 million, and neither KLB nor KDB would
provide more than $15 million equivalent of Bank subloans under the
loan to a single subborrower (para. 5.09).
Recommendation
6.03      A Bank loan of $222 million with a maturity of 15 years, including 3
years of grace is recommended to be made to the Republic of Korea.

REPUBLIC OF KOREA
SECOND INDUSTRIAL-.FINANCE PROJECT
The Minister's Financial Sector Policy Letter
Mr. A.W. Clausen, President                                         May 10, 1985
The World Bank
1818 H Street, N.W.
Washington, D.C. 20433
USA
Dear Mr. Clausen:
1.        I am  writing to inform you of changes in financial policy that have
occurred since my predecessor wrote to you in 1983 concerning financial sector
reform in Korea. That letter was provided in connection with the Industrial
Finance Project, and I am pleased to address this letter to the International
Bank for Reconstruction and Development for its appraisal of the Second
Industrial Finance Project.
2.        The Government has initiated a long range program of financial
liberalization that is expected to promote the development of the nationaL
economy by providing incentives for equity market development, resource mobi-
lization, and efficient allocation of credit. To achieve these long range
objectives, my Government seeks gradually (a) to enhance the role of market
forces in the determination of interest rates; (b) to increase competition
within the financial sector, and (c) to reduce the role of Government in cred-
it allocation.
3.        Our financial reforms are expected to (a) improve resource alloca-
tion by decentralizing decision-making so that financial institutions and
entrepreneurs can respond more flexibly to market forces; and (b) raise effi-
ciency in financial intermediation by subjecting intermediaries to greater
competition through freer entry and fewer legislative and administrative con-
straints on their activities. The benefits of these developments are expected
to include an increase in the supply of long term capital and a more effective
equity market that would enable more firms to raise capital from a broader
spectrum of savers.
4.        The long term objective of financial reform is clear, but the high
indebtedness of the corporate sector and the priority attached to strengthen-
ing the commercial banks constrain the pace of liberalization. Therefore,
reform is being implemented in a phased manner to minimize any potentially
destabilizing effects on the financial system and the corporate sector, while
efforts are being made to deal vith these constraints.



Annex 1
-40-                             Page 2 of 8
5.        This letter describes in detail the Government's objectives and its
planned course of action in major areas of policy reform. These reforms, pre-
sented below under five headings, will be directed toward both debt claims and
ownership claims. Policy formulation will seek to achieve an appropriate bal-
ance between these two broad forms of finance, which will require a more pro-
minent role for equity capital and a longer term structure of debt. Measures
that directly influence the price, composition and quantity of debt claims
include (a) interest rates and (b) directed credit. Measures that determine
the manner in which debt claims are generated include (c) liberalization and
supervision of commercial banks and (d) control of corporate indebtedness.
Ownership claims are treated as a separate issue (e) under the heading of
equity market development.
Decontrol of Interest Rates
6.        The Government's long-term objective as stated in the Fifth Five
Year Plan (1982-86) is to allow interest rates to be determined by market
forces. This objective should be achieved gradually in view of the volatility
of international interest rates, the heavy indebtedness of the corporate
sector and the persistence of the gap between official and market rates for
essentially similar instruments. The Government will use selective, incre-
mental adjustments to permit official rates to move more closely in line with
market rates for similar instruments as economic conditions and opportunities
permit. This should also help the development of an integrated array of rates
reflecting transaction costs, risks and the time value of money.
7.        In January 1984 the Government adjusted official interest rates to
lengthen the term structure of deposits, increase the effective spreads of
credit institutions, and provide scope for small differentiations in lending
rates to reflect risk and maturity. This reform included the following
changes in commercial bank interest rates: the time deposit rate for maturi-
ties exceeding one year was increased from 8% to 9%, short-term time and
savings deposit rates were lowered from 8% to 6%, and the lending rate was
changed from a uniform 10% to a 10% to 10.5% band. Commensurate changes were
made in other official rates specified for commercial banks and for non-bank
financial institutions. Subsequent changes in November 1984 increased major
lending rates and deposit rates by 1%, broadening the discretion of banks'
management by widening the commercial lending rate band from 10% to 11.5%. In
April 1985 the upper limit of the lending rate band was further increased to
13.5% for loans having a maturity in excess of one year. At the same time,
the rate structure on household savings deposits was altered to encourage
longer term deposits. The interest rate on these deposits having a maturity
in excess of six months was raised for 6% to 12%, and the rate on deposits
maturing in three to six months was raised from 6% to 9%.
8.        Rates are now generally positive: inflation was reduced in 1984 to
less than 3Z as measured by the consumer price index. The Government is comr
mitted to maintaining positive real rates of interest by keeping the rate on
time and saving deposits and on comnercial bank loans above the rate of inf-
lation as measured by the consummer price index. In the longer run, vith
liberalization, the present emphasis on Levels of nominal rates will naturally
be supplemented by greater concern for and attention to real rates in monetary



Annex I
- 41 -                           Page 3 of 8
policy and in private financial decision-making. The Government will seek to
ensure that those official rates that remain will be set in accordance with
trends in the domestic economy, the balance of payments and interest rates in
international financial markets. The Government is also comitted to keeping
a spread between the weighted cost of funds and official lending rates suffi-
cient to ensure the profitability of efficient commercial banks.
9.        The Government has adjusted the commercial bank deposit and lending
rates so as to reduce the differential between official rates and rates in the
second tier financial market (as evidenced, for example, by the reduction in
the differential between the one-year time deposit rate and the annual yield
on three-year corporate bonds from 6.2% at the end of 1983 to 4.7% at the end
of February 1985). The Government will continue to attempt to narrow the
differential by making strong efforts to keep the rate of inflation low and to
minimize inflationary expectations. These efforts will facilitate interest
rate decontrol, which would be achieved initially by gradually raising rate
ceilings and ultimately by removing controls entirely in order to permit the
market freely to determine deposit and lending rates of interest and related
terms and conditions. The Government expects that further adjustments in the
structure of commercial bank interest rates would be made in light of these
longer run objectives and current domestic and international circumstances.
Official rates and spreads for other types of financial institutions would be
changed accordingly in order not to discriminate sharply against any
particular type of institution.
10.       The Government has taken several steps to .increase the range of
services offered by financial institutions, to improve tie attractiveness of
financial assets, and to shift regulatory focus toward functions and away from
institutional type. Innovative financial instruments bearing higher rates of
interest have served as vehicles for these changes; one effect of their intro-
duction has been to bring afficial interest rates closer to market rates.
11.       The first of these was permitting all nationwide city banks to
undertake trust business, which was previously the preserve of a specialized
trust bank, and, since May 1983, also of local commercial banks. Trust busi-
ness involves term deposits placed by households and others. These funds are
accepted at rates ranging from 9% to 12.2% and loaned at 15.5% to 16.5Z,
producing positive spreads.
12.       Negotiable certificates of deposit were introduced on June 1, 1984.
These certificates may be issued by nationwide city banks, local banks and the
Korea Exchange Bank, and have maturities of from 91 to 180 days. Their unit
price is W100 million. They bear interest at no more than 11%, discounted in
advance. Ninety percent of funds raised by banks from this source must be
loaned by the issuing banks at 12%. In February 1985 the deposit and lending
rates were increased to 12% and 13%, respectively. These certificates have
received an overwhelming response in the market.
13.       For smaller savers, cash management accounts (CMA) were introduced
in April 1984. These accounts are offered by short term finance corporations,
have maturities of up to 180 days and are issued in units of at least W2 mil-
lion. The funds deposited in these accounts earn a floating rate and are used



- 42 -                           Annex 1
Page 4 of 8
to purchase instruments in the second tier market. More than half of these
portfolios must consist of debt issued by small and medium industries.
Government will continue to consider new instruments and measures (a) to
encourage greater long-term borrowing and lending and (b) to increase the
volume of financial savings.
Reduction of Directed Credit
14.       To increase the efficiency of resource allocation through financial
markete the Government wilL reduce the proportion of directed credit in total
domestic credit and reduce the use of private financial institutions as
conduits of concessional funds to preferred sectors. The share of commercial
banks in NIF disbursements will be progressively reduced, and efforts will be
made to transfer new NIF lending to speciali-ed banks.
15.       Net contributions to the National Investment Fund were reduced from
W 444 billion in 1983 to W 73 billion in 1984, and the volume of loans
extended was reduced from W 732 billion in 1983 to W 506 billion in 1984. The
size of the NIF will be further diminished by progressively reducing the vol-
ume of lending and phasing out obligatory contributions through deposits and
bond purchases. Otber special investment and guarantee funds established by
the Government to direct credit will also be limited to a declining portion of
total domestic credit. It is also the Government's intention that directed
credit will be provided at interest rates comparable to those charged
elsewhere in financial markets.
Comercial Bank Liberalization and Supervision
16.       The long-term objective of the Government is to enhance Korea's
economic growth by improving efficiency and competitiveness in the financial
sector. In the commercial banking subsector this will be achieved by
licensing new banks, expanding the powers of the regional banks, permitting
greater autonomy of bank management, and broadening the activities in which
banks are permitted to engage as a means of reducing enforced specializa-
tion. These changes have major implications for the manner in which banks are
supervised, and efforts are being made to enhance the supervisory function.
17.       Privatization of commercial banks has been completed, and a number
of new financial institutions have been chartered. The commercial banks have
improved their performance substantially in 1984 due in part to changes in
interest rate and fee structures, and from the expansion of their business
into new functions and through new instruments. The nationwide commercial
banks are active participants on both sides of international financial
markets. These banks are making efforts to develop their long range planning
mechanisms, internal controls and computerization of accounts.
18.       The Government is considering measures that would allow regional
commercial banks to expand their activities. Government will gradually
provide scope to specialized banks, including the Korea Development Bank and
the Korea Long Term Credit Bank, to engage in a wider range of services. With
respect to KDB, the Government expects eventually to segregate its funding and
accounts as a commercial entity from those as a government agent, and ensure



- 43                              Annex l
Page 5 of 8
that KDB in its commercial role does not compete with private financial
institutions by relying on preferential treatment from Government. The
Government has provided foreign bank branches in Korea greater access to the
domestic market, and has given them access to rediscount facilities with the
Bank of Korea.
19.       The Government expects that concessional rediscounting arrangements
for paper from preferred sectors will be gradually altered to coincide with
the terms prevailing for other eligible paper. This may require adjustments
in the rate and the level of refinancing currently available. These changes
will be made to encourage banks to operate more efficiently, and are expected
to facilitate the growth of an interbank funds market.
20.       Capital adequacy is being addressed through the supervisory mech-
anism applied to banks by the Bank of Korea. Capital adequ.cy is affected by
profitability and by the bad and doubtful loans carried by banks. With libe-
ralization, the Government seeks to demonstrate continued support for domestic
banks while easing their burden of bad and doubtful debts. Adjustments in
official interest rates and fees have augmented internally generated capital,
and the Government is taking further steps to improve the banks' capital ade-
quacy by increasing their profitability, and is considering additional mea-
sures that may be required to address portfolio quality problems.
21.       Any measures to regularize bad and doubtful loans would be intended
to permit the banks to increase their capital internally and through the issue
of new shares, and to facilitate their acceptance in international capital
markets on the basis of their own financial performance. Following this
reform, and with a significant decrease in direct Government involvement in
credit allocation, the Government would not expect to intervene to maintain
bank profits or confidence except in extreme cases affecting the nation's fin-
ancial structure.
22.       The Covernment has addressed the issue of the ownership of the
nationwide commercial banks by major corporations by limiting the voting
interest of any single shareholder to 8% of total shares outstanding. The
incentive for abuse of the special relationship between banks and their share-
holders is being dimini'hed by the move toward market rates of interest on
bank loans. Inspection procedures applied by the Office of Bank Supervision
and Examination seek to ensure that major shareholders are not given a.a undue
advantage over comparable bank clients through credit allocation and
charges. The relations between banks and their major corporate shareholders
will be kept under close review by supervisory authorities.
Control of Corporate Indebtedness
23.       The recession of 1980-82 highlighted the problem of corporate
indebtedness. In an effort to reduce the financial burden on Korean business
the Government lowered lending rates and certain corporate tax rates in June
1982.  It also took steps to revive the economy and reduce inflation.
Assisted by positive developments internationally, these measures have
returned the Korean economy to a high growth path and improved the profit-
ability of the corporate and financial sectors.



- 44 -                           Annex 1
Page 6 of 8
24.       The problem of corporate indebtedness is perhaps the most difficult
to resolve through liberalization. In a fully liberalized environment, the
issue of corporate indebtedness would be handled through market solutions to
problems and responses to opportunities. To deal with corporate indebtedness
while introducing liberalization, the Government does not expect to move
directly to recapitalize or-reorganize firms in financial difficulties except
where national security is concerned or where the nation's export performance
could be severely damaged. The Government's general policy is to deal with
these situations on a case-by-case basis.
25.       The problem of corporate indebtedness is being managed through the
prime bank system. The prime or lead bank for each large corporation centra-
lizes information about the corporation's borrowing and banking relation-
ships. It controls the manner in which commercial banks manage their large
clients' debt limits in conformity with Government guidelines under the close
scrutiny of the Office of Bank Supervision and Examination. The prime bank
system is working satisfactorily and helps to improve the financial structure
of corporations. However, in the longer run, those elements of the prime bank
system that restrict competition among the banks for attracting and serving
financially healthy clients would require modification. Liberalization
requires lending institutions to develop their own credit criteria, including
appropriate debt to equity and debt service guidelines. Credit quality mea-
sures could be further refined by independent, specialized credit rating and
information agencies.
26.       The control of corporate indebtendness by market forces will be
improved by disclosure measures discussed in this letter in connection with
equity market development. Consolidation of group financial statements,
emphasis on training of accountants, enhancing disclosure standards and
requiring issuers of commercial paper to file prospectuses annually will all
contribute to better management of corporate indebtedness in the public
interest.
Equity Market Development
27.       The development of the manufacturing and modern non-financial
services sectors in Korea has in most cases been much more dynamic than the
development of its financial instruments, the term to maturity of credit
markets and type of access to financial services available to many firms are
now perceived as a constraint to economic development. The part of the finan-
cial sector through which all of these problems are connected is the equity
market. An enhanced role for this market would help to make risk-reward rela-
tionships more transparent. It would also permit firms to adjust their finan-
cial structures to avoid some of the burden of debt, it could assist entrepre-
neurs seeking to raise funds and would permit greater private participation in
the capital gains produced by Korea's continued economic progress.
28.       The development of the stock market is fundamental to broadening the
financial base of Korean industry and to facilitating resource mobilization
for industrial investment through increased participation of individual savers
in the investment process. Accordingly, the Covernmint will continue its
efforts to make investment in securities (especially equities) more attractive



- 45 -                            Annex I
Page 7 of 8
by measures that will contribute to the quality and quantity of information
available to shareholders and facilitate equity capital issues by corpora-
tions. These efforts will include measures to enhance disclosure of corporate
finances and insider transactions.  To this end, the Government is considering
proposing revisions in the Security and Exchange Law. The Government will
continue to discourage intercompany equity and debt holdings. Also, the
Government will encourage large business groups to prepare consolidated fin-
ancial statements on a voluntary basis, and then consider gradually requiring
large groups to prepare consolidated financial statements. Covernment has
concentrated on maintaining confidence in the market and offering investors a
large pool of securities by requiring large enterprises to go public. These
efforts have included attention to listing and reporting requirements and have
resulted in the listing of more than 330 firms of the Korea Stock Exchange and
more than ten years of trading without any major irregularities.
29.       Diversification of investors' protfolios will be assisted by
continuing to permit the formation of unit trusts, mutual funds, and similar
vehicles in response to investor demand. Insurance companies will be encour-
aged to invest more heavily in securities through changes in investment guide-
lines. Foundations and pension funds will also be encouraged to buy securi-
ties. Special consideration will also be given to the importance of pension
funds for both social and investment purposes. Fiscal incentives to promote
contributions to pension funds will also be explored.
30.       The Government is comnitted to the use of fiscal incentives to
uromote shareholding more widely within Korea, in recognition of the great
social benefits that result from increased shareholding by the public. Steps
already taken in this direction include a reduction of corporate income tax
liability by 12Z of the value of new shares issued, in effect through 1986.
During 1985-86, the Committee on the Development of the Tax System will con-
sider, among other matters, fiscal incentives to encourage the development of
the equity market.
31.       Encouragement will be provided to the owners of family businesses to
go public. In late 1983 this was accomplished by reducing from 40X to 20% the
proportion of equity that had to be sold in an initial public offering. In
addition, the Government will promote plans to encourage small investors to
accumulate securities. For example, increases in limits governing participa-
tion in the Employees Security Savings Plan will be reviewed to encourage
greater shareholding by wage and salary earners.
32.       Efforts will increasingly be devoted to making the securities market
more dynamic by permitting market forces to play a larger role. A step was
taken in this direction in 1983 by permitting companies to make rights issues
at market value when market value exceeds par value. The Government will
CoWitinue its efforts to encourage private corporations to issue at market
price, and measures to ease current requirements will be considered.
33.       A more important change is that the Government no longer officially
or semiofficially influences the level of dividends to be paid by individual
firms or industrial segments. Firms are now free to declare dividends to
accommodate their internal financial situation and the expectation of their



46-                            Annex 1
Page 8 of 8
creditors and of their shareholders. This measure should help differentiate
investors' perceptions of the shares of listed companies and should create a
more dynamic stock market.
34.       The Government intends to permit revitalization of Korea's inactive
over-the-counter market under regulations enacted in 1984. OTC transactions
could benefit small firms and small investors, and could complement the
Government's efforts to direct resources towards small and medium industry.
OTC trading would be facilitated by the introduction of a "B list" of equities
traded on the Korea Stock Exchange, subject to less rigorous listing require-
ments than presently applied. Current listings would constitute the "A list"
under the revised listing categories. Introduction of OTC trading on the
Exchange will minimize any fragmentation that may arise from different listing
requirements.
35.       The Korea Stock Exchange will also be used increasingly to attract
foreign portfolio investment for Korean enterprises. Internationalization of
the market began with the formation of several investment trusts permitted to
offer shares to offshore investors. A major step towards further internatio-
nalization was taken in September 1984 with the formation of the Korea Fund
and the listing of its shares on the New York Stock Exchange. Further steps
to attract portfoLio investment will include the development of new instru-
ments designed especially for this purpose. Steps have been taken to permit
qualified foreign securities firms to open representative offices, and Korean
securities firms have been approached by foreign securities firms seeking
minority shareholdings.
36.       I would be pleased to inform the World Bank of our progress in the
implementation of financial reforms.
Yours sincerely,
Minister of Finance
